جواب:۔ یہ ہے کہ باری تعالیٰ کے اس ارشاد کا حکم شرعی نہ ہونا ممنوع ہے حق یہ ہے کہ یہ آیت حکم شرعی سے کیونکہ آیت متلوہ لا تعبدوا شيئاً من دون الله وانكم وماتعبدون من دونه حصب جهنم کے معنی میں ہے اور اس میں شک نہیں کہ اس تقدیر پر آیت متلوہ حکم ہے کہ اس میں طلب ترک اور اقتضاء ضمنی موجود ہے۔
مندرجہ بالا دونوں اعتراض کے دفع کے لئے مائن نے حکم کی تعریف میں اقتضاء او خیر ا کا اضافہ کیا کہ اعتراض اول اس لئے واقع نہ ہوگا کہ اباحت ، ندب اور کراہت میں تکلیف متحقق ہے اور انکم و ما تعبدون الایہ میں ضمنا طلب ہے اس لئے حکم میں شامل ہی ہے رہی ، والله خلقکم و ماتعملون الا یہ تو یہ آیت حکم میں داخل ہی نہیں ہے کہ اس میں اقتضاء و خیر نہیں ہے بلکہ اس آیت میں فعل عبد کی حالت کی خبر ہے اور اقتضاء وتخیر یاحکم از قبیل انشاء ہے۔
Objection: Allah's saying 'And Allah created you and what you do' and similarly 'Creator of everything' and other blessed verses are Allah's addresses and are also related to the actions of servants. Therefore, such verses enter within the definition of hukm. So the definition of hukm is no longer preventing the entry of others.
Answer: This is that in every definition, a condition of perspective (haythiyya) is considered, even if the specification of perspective is not explicit in it. Thus perspective is considered in the definition of hukm. Therefore, the full phrase is: 'The address of Allah Almighty related to the action of the responsible person, insofar as he is responsible, etc.' So the objection regarding preventiveness will not affect the definition of hukm, because in the presented verses, the address is not related from the perspective that it is an action of a servant.
However, two objections have been raised regarding considering the condition of perspective in the definition of hukm. First reason: Allama Taftazani said in his commentary on Mukhtasar that considering the aspect of obligation in the address being related to the servant's action of permission, recommendation, and disapproval is subject to consideration.
Answer: This is that obedience to the divine command occurs in two ways. One form of obedience to the divine command is to hold the belief that it is true regarding that command. The second form of obedience is to act according to the requirement of the command. So in the ruling of permissibility, although the demand is conditional on the will of the responsible person, and in the permissible act there is no concept of obligation, nevertheless, belief in permissibility in the permissible act is definitely obligatory. Therefore, in the ruling of permissibility, obligation is realized in terms of belief in truth; the same applies to recommendation and disapproval. Thus, in a recommended act, belief in its being recommended is obligatory, and in a disapproved act, belief in its being disapproved is obligatory. Rather, in both, obligation is realized in another respect as well, in that in both (recommended and disapproved) there is obligation with a preferential demand: in the first, for performing the recommended act, reward will be given; in the second, for committing the disapproved act, punishment will be inflicted. Second reason: The second objection regarding considering the mentioned perspective in the definition of hukm is made by Sayyid Muhaqqiq: that Allah's saying 'Indeed, you and those you worship besides Allah are firewood of Hell' is a threat that is attached to the action of the responsible person as an action of the responsible person. Therefore, it should be a hukm, whereas by consensus it is not a shari ruling. But by considering the perspective, entry into hukm becomes necessary; thus the definition of hukm does not prevent the entry of others.
Answer: This is that the denial that this saying of the Almighty is a shari ruling is prohibited. The truth is that this verse is a shari ruling because the recited verse 'Do not worship anything besides Allah, and indeed you and those you worship besides Allah are firewood of Hell' is in that meaning, and there is no doubt that on this assumption the recited verse is a hukm because it contains a demand for abandonment and an implicit requirement.
To repel the above two objections, Ma'een added 'demand or choice' to the definition of hukm. The first objection will not occur because in permissibility, recommendation, and disapproval, obligation is realized, and in 'Indeed you and what you worship besides Allah' there is an implicit demand, so it is included in hukm. As for 'And Allah created you and what you do', that verse is not included in hukm because there is no demand or choice in it; rather, that verse is a report about the state of the servant's action, and demand, choice, or hukm are of the category of creations (insha').
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the definition of hukm as 'خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف من حيث هو مكلف' (the address of Allah related to the action of the responsible person insofar as he is responsible) and their responses. The first objection: verses like 'والله خلقكم و ما تعملون' (And Allah created you and what you do) are addresses of Allah related to actions of servants, so they would enter the definition, making it non-preventive of others. The response: every definition considers a specific perspective (haythiyya), even if not stated explicitly. The full definition includes 'من حيث هو مكلف' (insofar as he is responsible); these verses do not address actions from that perspective. The second objection: the verse 'إنكم و ما تعبدون من دون الله حصب جهنم' (Indeed you and what you worship besides Allah are firewood of Hell) is a threat attached to actions as such, so it should be a ruling, but by consensus it is not. The response: this verse implies a prohibition (demand to abandon), so it contains an implicit demand (iqtida' damini), making it a hukm. The commentator (Ma'een) adds that including 'اقتضاء أو تخير' (demand or choice) in the definition resolves both objections: the first because permissibility, recommendation, and disapproval involve obligation in belief or preferential demand; the second because the verse contains implicit demand. The verse about creation is purely reportive and lacks demand or choice, so it is not included. The passage thus defends the definition and clarifies the role of perspective and implicit demand.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف من حيث هو مكلف (the address of Allah related to the action of the responsible person insofar as he is responsible)
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Demand or requirement present in the address, either explicit (sarih) or implicit (damni).
تخير (takhyir): Choice or option, referring to rulings that allow choice (like permissibility).
حيثية (haythiyya): Perspective or aspect; a qualifier in definitions that specifies the relevant angle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the verse 'والله خلقكم وما تعملون' (And Allah created you and what you do) not considered a hukm?
A) Because it is not an address of Allah.
B) Because it is related to the action of the servant from the perspective of obligation.
C) Because it is related to the action of the servant not from the perspective of obligation, but as a report, and it lacks demand or choice.
D) Because it contains an implicit demand.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm, refined by adding 'demand or choice', correctly excludes reportive verses and includes all legislative addresses.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The First Investigation: Does the Definition of Hukm Include Prescriptive Rulings?
Discusses whether ahkam wadi'iyyah are excluded from the definition of hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three scholarly responses to the exclusion of prescriptive rulings from the definition of hukm and evaluate which position is preferred in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The definition of hukm excludes prescriptive rulings; scholars differ on whether and how to include them.

In the previous lesson, we defined hukm as Allah's address concerning the action of the responsible person. However, this definition seems to cover only taklifi rulings (commands, prohibitions). What about prescriptive rulings (ahkam wadi'iyyah) like causes, conditions, and impediments? This passage presents the first investigation (bahth) of the definition, where scholars discuss whether these rulings are included and what should be done about it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
The definition of hukm given in the matn is not universal (لا ينعكس) because it excludes prescriptive rulings (ahkam wadi'iyyah). Recognize that the definition uses 'iqtida'' (demand), which prescriptive rulings do not contain.
Prescriptive rulings include conditions, causes, and impediments.
2
Examine the First Response: Add 'وضعاً'
Some usul scholars add the word 'وضعاً' (prescriptive) to the definition to explicitly include prescriptive rulings. This modifies the definition to 'the address of Allah related to the action of the responsible person insofar as he is responsible, prescriptively.'
This approach expands the definition to be universal.
3
Examine the Second Response: Implicit Demand
Others argue that prescriptive rulings are not excluded because 'iqtida'' (demand) is general enough to include both explicit and implicit demand. Prescriptive rulings, though not explicit demands, involve an implicit demand (e.g., the condition of wudu implicitly demands that if you want to pray, you must have wudu). Thus, the definition already includes them.
They rely on the generality of the term 'iqtida''.
4
Examine the Third Response: Terminological Limitation
A third group simply refuses to call prescriptive rulings 'hukm' in the technical sense. They say that in our madhhab, 'hukm' is reserved for taklifi rulings. If others call them hukm, it is a terminological difference, not a substantive one.
This avoids the need to modify the definition.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
While the first two responses address the inclusion, the Hanafi school generally adheres to the third position: prescriptive rulings are not considered hukm in the same way. The definition stands as given for taklifi rulings only. This is a valid terminological choice and does not affect the substance of fiqh.
The passage concludes 'there is no dispute' (ولا مشاحة) regarding the third view.

Text of al-Muslim: 'And here are investigations: the first is that it is not universal because prescriptive rulings are excluded from it. Some of them added 'and prescriptive' and some did not add. Sometimes he prevents their exclusion from the definition because iqtida' (demand) is more general than explicit and implicit, and a story as a story has no demand in it. What is in al-Tahrir that the prescriptive is prior to demand does not harm due to the truth of the more general. And sometimes he prevents them from being among the defined, for we do not call it a ruling even if others call it so, and there is no dispute.'
Translation: And here there are several investigations. The first investigation is that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive, because prescriptive rulings (ahkam wadi'iyyah) are excluded from this definition. Therefore, some usul scholars added 'and prescriptive' (و وضعاً), and some did not add. But they prevent the exclusion of prescriptive rulings from the definition because iqtida' (demand) is more general than explicit and implicit, and a story as a story has no demand in it. What is in al-Tahrir that the prescriptive is prior to demand does not harm due to the truth of the more general. And sometimes they prevent them from being among the defined, for we do not call it a ruling even if others call it so, and there is no dispute.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the first investigation (bahth) regarding the definition of hukm: the definition given earlier (the address of Allah related to the action of the responsible person insofar as he is responsible) is not universal (لا ينعكس) because it excludes prescriptive rulings (ahkam wadi'iyyah), such as causes, conditions, and impediments. Scholars have two approaches: some add 'وضعاً' (prescriptive) to the definition to include them. Others do not add but argue that prescriptive rulings are not actually excluded because the term 'iqtida'' (demand) in the definition is general and includes both explicit and implicit demand; prescriptive rulings involve implicit demand. A third position is that prescriptive rulings are simply not called 'hukm' in this madhhab, but there is no harm if others call them so. The passage thus illustrates the internal Hanafi debate on the scope of the term 'hukm'.

Key Terms
احكام وضعية (ahkam wadi'iyyah): Prescriptive rulings that establish causes, conditions, impediments, etc., as opposed to taklifi rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.).
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Demand, which can be explicit (sarih) or implicit (damni).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first investigation about the definition of hukm?
A) It is not universal because it excludes prescriptive rulings.
B) It is not specific because it includes non-divine addresses.
C) It is not consistent because of the condition of perspective.
D) It is not accepted by all scholars.

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm in Hanafi usul is primarily for taklifi rulings; prescriptive rulings are either included via implicit demand or excluded terminologically, and the latter is the preferred view.

Defining Hukm: Inclusion of Ahkam Wad'iyyah via Implicit Demand
Usul definition with expansion and objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul position that iqtida' dimni includes ahkam wad'iyyah in the definition of hukm, and distinguish between the two responses to the exclusion objection.

Core Idea
Ahkam wad'iyyah are included in hukm because they contain implicit demand, even if not explicit.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a critical challenge to the definition of hukm: it seems to exclude ahkam wad'iyyah (declaratory rulings). Two responses are given: one adds 'wad'an' to the definition, the other relies on the broader concept of iqtida' that includes implicit demand. Understanding these responses is key to grasping the Hanafi usul framework.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish taklifi and wad'i rulings
Identify the two types of hukm: original/taklifi (e.g., obligation of prayer) and declaratory/wad'i (e.g., cause, condition). The definition must encompass both.
Wad'i rulings are subordinate to taklifi.
2
Distinguish explicit and implicit demand
Know that iqtida' has two forms: sarihi (explicit, e.g., 'Establish prayer') and dimni (implicit, e.g., 'The sun has declined' implies obligation).
Implicit demand is understood from context.
3
Understand the objection
The definition of hukm as 'address involving demand or option' seems to exclude wad'i rulings because they lack explicit demand. The objector argues that statements like 'The sun's decline is a cause for prayer' are not demands.
This is the first investigation's objection.
4
Analyze the first response: adding 'wad'an'
Some usul scholars add the condition 'wad'an' (as a placement) to explicitly include wad'i rulings, making the definition: 'Allah's address – demand-based, option-based, or placement-based – relating to the servant's action.'
This ensures inclusion but changes the wording.
5
Analyze the second response: iqtida' covers implicit demand
Other scholars (including Imam Razi and Subki) hold that 'iqtida'' in the definition already includes implicit demand. Thus, wad'i rulings are already covered without adding 'wad'an'. For example, 'The sun's decline is a cause for prayer' implicitly demands obligation.
This is the preferred Hanafi view.

Elaboration - His saying: 'And here there are investigations. First, that it does not invert, for it excludes...' Despite restricting the definition of hukm with the conditions of demand and option, the definition of the lowly one could not be devoid of defect; rather, despite that, four strong objections are brought against the definition of hukm. The author mentioned them in four investigations with responses. In the above phrase, the first investigation's objection with its response is mentioned. Before understanding the objection and response, keep two things in mind. First thing: Hukm is of two types: (1) Original hukm or intended-for-itself hukm, (2) Declaratory hukm or not-intended-for-itself hukm. (1) Original hukm (taklifi hukm) is called what is independently legislated primarily, not legislated as subordinate within another hukm, like the very obligation of prayer. (2) Declaratory hukm is called what the Lawgiver has placed as subordinate to an independent hukm, in the sense that it is the cause, condition, or proof of the independent hukm, like the declining of the sun which is a cause for the obligation of prayer, and like purification which is a condition for the validity of prayer. Both examples (declining of sun and purification) are cause and condition for the independent hukm of prayer, i.e., obligation. So the obligatory hukm of prayer is the original hukm, and the declining of the sun and purification are declaratory hukm for it, as they are its cause and condition. Second thing: Demand is also of two types: (1) Explicit demand, (2) Implicit demand. (1) Explicit demand: That towards which there is clear evidence of demand and request, like the command of Allah: 'Complete the prayer' is explicit text for the demand of establishing prayer. (2) Implicit demand: That towards which there is no clear explicit evidence of demand, like the demand of the declining of the sun for the obligation of prayer. After keeping these two things in mind, now understand the first investigation's objection and its response. Formulation of the objection: It is that despite restricting the definition of hukm with the condition of demand and option, the definition is not comprehensive because from the mentioned definition, the declaratory rulings which the Lawgiver has made subordinate to original rulings as cause, condition, pillar, or preventer would necessarily be excluded, such as the address that the declining of the sun is a cause for the obligation of prayer, purification is a condition for the validity of prayer, recitation is a pillar for prayer, and impurity is a preventer of prayer, while these are unanimously rulings. Response: Its formulation is that to avoid this objection, some usul scholars added the condition 'wad'an' (as a placement) in the definition of hukm, i.e., declaratory rulings are all placed by the Lawgiver. So if the declining of the sun is a cause for the obligation of prayer, it is only due to the Lawgiver's placement. Similarly, with the addition of 'wad'an', the definition of hukm would be: Hukm is the address of Allah—demand-based, option-based, or placement-based—which relates to the action of the servant. Obviously, according to this definition, declaratory rulings would be included in hukm. His saying: 'And among them is one who did not add, so sometimes he prevents its exclusion...' This is like a second response to the previous objection. The gist of this response is that some usul scholars, like Imam Razi and Imam Subki, did not add the condition 'wad'an'. They claim that the objection from declaratory rulings does not arise, because declaratory rulings are not excluded from the definition. That is because the 'demand' mentioned in the definition includes both explicit and implicit demand. So when demand was mentioned, it covers both types. Hence, there is no need to mention the condition 'wad'an' to include declaratory rulings, because even though in declaratory addresses there is no explicit demand, there is implicit demand in them. Consider the time of declining sun is a cause for prayer, whose meaning is: 'Prayer is obligatory at the time of declining sun.' So in that, even though the demand for the obligation of prayer is not explicitly mentioned,
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of hukm (ruling) from the matn, addressing objections regarding the inclusion of ahkam wad'iyyah (declaratory rulings). The sharh first clarifies two preliminary distinctions: (1) the division of hukm into original/taklifi and declaratory/wad'i, and (2) the division of iqtida' (demand) into explicit and implicit. These distinctions are crucial for understanding the objection and its responses. The objection states that the definition of hukm, which restricts to demand and option, excludes declaratory rulings because there is no evident demand in them. The first response, adopted by some scholars, is to add the qayd of 'wad'an' (placement) to the definition, explicitly including the Lawgiver's placement. The second response, held by Imam Razi and Imam Subki, argues that the term 'iqtida'' itself covers both explicit and implicit demand, so declaratory rulings are already included. The passage then begins to explain this by giving the meaning of a declaratory statement like 'The declining sun is a cause for prayer' as implying the obligation of prayer at that time, thereby involving implicit demand. The commentary remains Hanafi throughout, highlighting that the Hanafi school accepts both types of demand as sufficient for the definition of hukm.

Key Terms
حکم وضعی (hukm wad'i): A ruling placed by the Lawgiver as a cause, condition, or sign for an original ruling.
اقتضاء ضمنی (iqtida' dimni): Implicit demand not directly expressed but understood from context.
اقتضاء صریحی (iqtida' sarihi): Explicit demand clearly expressed in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second response (without adding 'wad'an'), why are ahkam wad'iyyah not excluded from the definition of hukm?
A) Because they contain explicit demand like taklifi rulings.
B) Because they contain implicit demand (iqtida' dimni).
C) Because they are causes for taklifi rulings.
D) Because they are not considered hukm by anyone.

Key Takeaway
Ahkam wad'iyyah are included in hukm because they entail implicit demand (iqtida' dimni), as per Hanafi usul.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Including Aḥkām Wāḍiʿah in the Definition of Ḥukm
Defense of including declaratory rulings in the definition of ḥukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that aḥkām wāḍiʿah are included in the definition of ḥukm and evaluate the two responses to the objection of exclusion.

Core Idea
Aḥkām wāḍiʿah are ḥukm either by adding 'wāḍʿan' or via implicit demand (iqtiḍā' manawī).

The definition of ḥukm as 'the speech of Allah concerning the actions of the servants that demands or gives a choice' seems to exclude declaratory rulings like cause, condition, pillar, and preventer. This passage presents two Hanafi responses: some add the word 'wāḍʿan' to the definition, while others rely on the generality of iqtiḍā' to include implicit demand. We will dissect these arguments and a further objection about Quranic stories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition and the objection
State the common definition of ḥukm as 'the speech of Allah that demands or gives a choice'. Then identify why aḥkām wāḍiʿah (e.g., cause, condition) appear to be excluded: they do not directly command or give a choice.
The objection is that the definition is not comprehensive.
2
Understand the first response: adding 'wāḍʿan'
Some uṣūliyyūn added the condition 'wāḍʿan' (placed) to the definition, so ḥukm includes all speech placed by the Lawgiver, both original and declaratory. This ensures aḥkām wāḍiʿah are included.
This approach modifies the definition explicitly.
3
Understand the second response: implicit demand
Other scholars (Imam Razi, Subki) claim that the word 'iqtiḍā'' in the definition covers both explicit (ṣarīḥī) and implicit (manawī) demand. Declaratory statements implicitly demand a ruling; e.g., 'the sun's declination is a cause for prayer' implies 'prayer is obligatory at that time'. Thus no addition is needed.
The implicitness is inherent, not requiring external evidence.
4
Analyze the further objection and its answer
If iqtiḍā' includes implicit demand, then Quranic stories (e.g., tales of past nations) would also be rulings because they implicitly demand lessons. The answer distinguishes: a story as a mere narrative has no iqtiḍā'; only when combined with an external evidence (like 'so take heed') does it become an implicit demand. Thus the definition remains exclusive.
This refines the concept of iqtiḍā' manawī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جیسے دلوک شمس کا اقتضاء وجوب صلوۃ کے لئے۔ ان دونوں باتوں کو ذہن نشین کرنے کے بعد اب بحث اول کا اعتراض اور اسکا جواب سمجھئے۔ اعتراض کی تقریر :۔ یہ ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف کو اقتضاء اور تخیر کی قید سے مقید کرنے کے باوجود حکم کی تعریف جامع نہیں ہے کیونکہ حد مذکور سے احکام وضعیہ کو شارع نے احکام اصلیہ کے تابع کر کے سبب و شرط ، یار کن اور مانع وغیرہ کے طور پر ذکر کیا ہے“ کا خروج لازم آرہا ہے جیسے یہ خطاب کہ دلوک شمس وجوب صلوۃ کا سبب ہے، طہارت صحت صلوٰۃ کے لئے شرط ہے اور قرآت نماز کے لئے رکن اور نجاست صلوۃ کے مانع ہے حالانکہ یہ بالا تفاق احکام ہیں۔ جواب : کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ اسی اعتراض مذکور سے احتراز کے لئے بعض اصولین نے حکم کی تعریف میں ووضعا“ کی قید کا اضافہ کیا ہے کہ احکام وضعیہ سب شارع کے وضع کردہ ہیں لہذا دلوک مشمس اگر وجوب صلوۃ کا سبب ہوا ہے تو وضع شارع ہی کی وجہ سے ہے علی ھذا القیاس پس ووضعا‘ کی قید اضافی کی صورت میں حکم کی تعریف اس طرح ہوگی کہ حکم عبارت ہے اللہ عز شانہ کے اس خطاب اقتضائی ، اتخییری یا وضعی کا جو فعل عبد سے متعلق ہو۔ ظاہر ہے کہ اس تعریف کے مطابق احکام وضعیہ حکم میں داخل رہیں گے۔ قوله ومنهم من لم يزد فتارة يمنع خروجها الخ . اعتراض سابق کا گویا یہ دوسرا جواب ہے اس جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ بعض علماء اصول جیسے امام رازی اور امام سبکی وغیرہ نے وضعا " کی قید نہیں لگائی ہے ان لوگوں کا دعوی ہے کہ احکام وضعیہ سے اعتراض وارد نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ احکام وضعیہ حد سے خارج ہی نہیں ہیں اس لئے کہ حکم کی تعریف میں جو اقتضاء مذکور ہے وہ اقتضاء صریکی واقتضاء فیمنی دونوں سے عام ہے پس جب اقتضاء ذکر کر دیا گیا تو دونوں قسموں کو عام اور شامل ہو گیا لہذا اب بار دیگر احکام وضعیہ کو داخل کرنے کے لئے قید وضع کے ذکر کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں رہی کہ خطابات وضعیہ میں گرچہ اقتضاء صریحی نہیں پائی جاتی لیکن اس میں اقتضاء منی ضرور پائی جاتی ہے۔ دیکھو وقت دلوک شمس نماز کے لئے سبب ہے جس کا معنی "الصلوة واجبة عند دلوک الشمس ہے پس اس میں گرچہ نماز کے لئے اقتضاء وقت کا وجوب صراحت مذکور نہیں ہے تاہم اقتضاء وقت کا وجوب لازماً اس سے مفہوم ہوتا ہے گو کہ وجوب صلوٰۃ کا حکم ، وجوب اقتضاء وقت کے حکم کا غیر ہے مگر یہ غیریت وجوب منمنی کے منافی نہیں ہے۔ حاشیہ میں ہے۔ ای سلمنا انه غير الاقتضاء لكنه متضمن له لان سببية الزنا لوجوب الحد ففي قوة وجوب الحد عند الزنا وعليه فقس یعنی ہم کو تسلیم ہے کہ اقتضاء وجوب صلوۃ اقتضاء وجوب وقت کا غیر ہے لیکن وہ اس کو متضمن ہے اس لئے کہ وجوب حد کے لئے زنا کا سبب ہونا عند الزنا وجوب حد کے منزل میں ہے اسی پر احکام وضعیہ کو قیاس کرنا چاہئے ۔ قوله والقصة من حيث هي القصة لا اقتضاء فيها درج بالا جواب پر ایک اعتراض وارد ہوتا ہے۔ ماتن نے اس عبارت سے اسی اعتراض کا جواب دیا ہے۔ اعتراض کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ اقتضاء کو عام ماننے پر حکم کی تعریف اگر چه احکام وضعیہ کو شامل ہو کر جامع ہو جاتی ہے لیکن اب حکم کی تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہ رہے گی کیونکہ اقتضاء کی عمومیت کی قصص احکام میں داخل ہوا چاہتے ہیں اس لئے کہ قصص میں اقتضا نمنی پائی جاتی ہے چنانچہ قصص و اساطیر سے مفہوم ہوتا ہے کہ اصحاب قصہ کے دردناک و ہلاکت خیز داستان کو یا ان کے حسن عمل اور اتباع شریعت کی پیروی کو دیکھ کر افعال سیئہ سے اجتناب کیا جائے اور اعمال صالحہ کی بجا آوری کی تقديري جائے اور یہی اقتضا میمنی ہے حالانکہ اصولین نے نقص کو احکام سے نہیں گردانا ہے ۔ جواب : کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ قصہ کے دو اعتبارات ہیں اسکا ایک اعتبار یہ ہے کہ وہ اقوام کی ہلاکت خیز داستان ، ان کی تباہیوں کے المناک واقعات ہیں یا انکے حسین کارنامہ کی حکایت ہے اس اعتبار سے اس میں صراحتہ ضمنا مطلقاً اقتضا نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اقتضا نمنی کی دلالت کا معنی یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اس پر لفظ بغیر دلیل خارجی کے غیر صریحی طور پر دلالت کرے۔ اور قصہ کا دوسرا اعتبار یہ ہے کہ وہ قصہ کسی دلیل خارجی مثلاً فاعتبروایا اولی الابصار کے سبب
Such as the declination of the sun indicating the obligation of prayer. After memorizing both matters, now understand the objection to the first discussion and its answer. The objection: The definition of hukm, despite being restricted by the condition of iqtiḍā' and takhyīr, is not comprehensive because declaratory rulings (aḥkām wāḍiʿah), which the Lawgiver has made subordinate to original rulings as cause, condition, pillar, or preventer, are excluded. For example, the statement that the declination of the sun is a cause for the obligation of prayer, that purity is a condition for the validity of prayer, that recitation is a pillar of prayer, and that impurity is a preventer of prayer—yet these are unanimously rulings. The answer: To avoid this objection, some uṣūliyyūn have added the condition 'wāḍʿan' to the definition of hukm, i.e., declaratory rulings are all placed by the Lawgiver. So if the declination of the sun is a cause for obligation, it is by the Lawgiver's placement. Thus with the addition of 'wāḍʿan', the definition becomes: hukm is the speech of Allah Almighty that is iqtiḍā'ī, takhyīrī, or wāḍʿī, related to the action of the servant. According to this definition, declaratory rulings are included in hukm. His statement: 'And among them are those who did not add [wāḍʿan], and sometimes they deny its exclusion...' This is the second answer to the previous objection. Its gist is that some uṣūl scholars like Imam Rāzī and Imam Subkī did not add the condition 'wāḍʿan'. They claim that the objection from declaratory rulings does not arise because they are not excluded from the definition. For the iqtiḍā' mentioned in the definition is general, covering both explicit iqtiḍā' (ṣarīḥī) and implicit iqtiḍā' (manawī). When iqtiḍā' is mentioned, it becomes general and includes both types. Thus there is no need to mention 'wāḍʿ' again to include declaratory rulings, because although declaratory addresses do not contain explicit iqtiḍā', they necessarily contain implicit iqtiḍā'. Consider the time of the declination of the sun as a cause for prayer, whose meaning is 'prayer is obligatory at the declination of the sun'. Although the explicit iqtiḍā' of the time's obligation for prayer is not mentioned, the implicit iqtiḍā' of the time's obligation is necessarily understood. Even though the hukm of the obligation of prayer is other than the hukm of the obligation of the time's iqtiḍā', this otherness does not negate implicit obligation. In the gloss: 'We concede that it is other than the iqtiḍā', but it implies it, because the causality of zinā for the obligation of hadd is in the power of the obligation of hadd at the time of zinā, and upon this analogize.' His statement: 'And the story, in itself as a story, has no iqtiḍā' in it.' An objection arises to the above answer. The author has answered this objection with this statement. The objection: Even if the definition becomes comprehensive with the generality of iqtiḍā', it will no longer prevent the inclusion of non-rulings, because the generality of iqtiḍā' would cause stories to enter into rulings, since stories contain implicit iqtiḍā'. For example, from stories and fables it is understood that seeing the painful and destructive tales of the people of the story or their good deeds and following of the sharī'ah, one should avoid evil deeds and perform good deeds—this is implicit iqtiḍā'. Yet uṣūliyyūn have not considered stories to be rulings. The summary of the answer: The story has two aspects. One aspect: it is simply a tale of destruction or good deeds; in this aspect, there is absolutely no explicit or implicit iqtiḍā', because the meaning of implicit iqtiḍā' is that the word indicates it non-explicitly without an external evidence. The second aspect: the story, due to an external evidence like 'So take heed, O people of insight', becomes indicative.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of hukm (ruling) in Uṣūl al-Fiqh, specifically addressing whether aḥkām wāḍiʿah (declaratory rulings such as cause, condition, pillar, preventer) are included in the definition. The author presents a common objection: the standard definition of hukm as 'the speech of Allah concerning the actions of the servants that demands or gives a choice' seems to exclude declaratory rulings, since they do not directly demand or give choice. For example, the statement 'the declination of the sun is a cause for the obligation of prayer' does not itself command prayer; it merely establishes a cause. Yet scholars agree that such statements are rulings. The first response, by some uṣūliyyūn, is to add the word 'wāḍʿan' (placed, established) to the definition, so that hukm covers both original and declaratory rulings as they are placed by the Lawgiver. The second response, attributed to Imām Rāzī and Imām Subkī, is that the word 'iqtiḍā'' (demand) in the definition includes both explicit demand (ṣarīḥī) and implicit demand (manawī). Declaratory rulings, though not explicitly commanding, implicitly demand because they entail a ruling. For instance, 'the sun's declination is a cause for prayer' implicitly means 'prayer is obligatory at that time'. Thus, the definition already includes declaratory rulings without adding 'wāḍʿan'. The author further defends this by citing a gloss that such implicitness is valid. Then a further objection arises: if iqtiḍā' is taken to include implicit demand, then stories in the Quran (like tales of past nations) would also be rulings, because they implicitly demand that we learn lessons. The answer distinguishes two aspects of a story: as a mere narrative, it has no iqtiḍā'; but when combined with an external evidence (like 'so take heed'), it becomes an implicit demand. The author thus refines the definition by clarifying that iqtiḍā' manawī exists only when the word itself, without external evidence, carries a non-explicit indication. This is a typical Hanafi usul discussion, emphasizing precise definition and distinguishing between inherent and externally-supported implication.

Key Terms
حکم (ḥukm): The speech of Allah concerning the actions of the servants that either demands, gives a choice, or is declaratory (placed).
اقتضاء صریحی (iqtiḍā' ṣarīḥī): Explicit demand where the wording directly commands or prohibits.
اقتضاء منوی (iqtiḍā' manawī): Implicit demand where the meaning necessarily follows from the wording without explicit mention.
احکام وضعیة (aḥkām wāḍiʿah): Declaratory rulings: cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), pillar (rukn), preventer (māniʿ).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do Imam Razi and Imam Subki respond to the objection that declaratory rulings are excluded from the definition of ḥukm?
A) They add the word 'wāḍʿan' to the definition.
B) They argue that iqtiḍā' covers both explicit and implicit demand, so declaratory rulings are already included.
C) They exclude declaratory rulings from the definition of ḥukm.
D) They claim declaratory rulings are not rulings at all.

Key Takeaway
Aḥkām wāḍiʿah are included in the definition of ḥukm either by adding 'wāḍʿan' or through the generality of iqtiḍā' covering implicit demand.

Two Meanings of Iqtida' and Inclusion of Wad'
Response to objection: wad' included in general iqtida'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two meanings of iqtida' (specific and general) and apply the Hanafi principle that general iqtida' includes wad'i rulings without circularity.

Core Idea
Iqtida' in the definition of hukm is general, encompassing both explicit and implicit implications, thus wad' is included without circularity.

An objection is raised: if wad' (positional rulings) is part of iqtida', then the definition of hukm would be circular because the prior (wad') would be included in the later (iqtida'). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response: iqtida' has two meanings. The general meaning includes both explicit and implicit implications, avoiding the circularity. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting the Hanafi definition of hukm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if wad' is within iqtida', then the prior (wad') is included in the later (iqtida'), which is impossible. Understand the logical concern: inclusion of a prior in a later would be circular.
The objection is from al-Tahrir, representing a critique of the Hanafi definition.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of iqtida'
The author states iqtida' has two meanings: specific (خاص) and general (عام). Specific iqtida' applies only to explicit implications; general iqtida' covers both explicit and implicit (including wad'). Identify which meaning is used in the definition of hukm.
The general iqtida' is the one intended, not the specific.
3
Analyze the inclusion claim
The author clarifies that including wad' in general iqtida' does not entail the prior (wad') being included in the later (iqtida') in a problematic sense. Rather, there is a common meaning that encompasses both. The prior qua prior is not included.
This resolves the circularity objection.
4
Apply the distinction
When reading Hanafi usul texts, when you see 'iqtida' in the definition of hukm, remember it means general iqtida'. This affects how you understand the scope of hukm: it includes both taklifi and wad'i rulings.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul and guards against misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پر مقدم ہوگا تو پھر یہ کیونکر کہا جاسکتا ہے کہ وضع ، اقتضاء میں داخل ہے کہ مقدم ، مؤخر میں داخل نہیں ہوتا ۔ جواب :۔ دیتے ہوئے کہتے ہیں کہ جس چیز کو ہم ثابت کرنے کے درپے ہیں تحریر کا اعتراض ہمارے لئے مضر نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اقتضاء کا دو معنی ہوتا ہے ایک اقتضاء خاص ۔ دوسرا اقتضاء عام ، اور اقتضاء عام صریحی و ضمنی دونوں قسموں کو عام ہوتا ہے جبکہ اقتضاء خاص اسکے برعکس صرف اقتضاء صریحی پر ہی بولا جاتا ہے اور حکم کی تعریف میں جس اقتضاء کا ذکر ہے وہ اقتضاء عام ہے جو صریحی و ضمنی (وضع ) کو عام و شامل ہے رہا اقتضاء خاص جسکا اطلاق صرف صریحی پر ہوتا ہے مراد نہیں ہے۔
حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ مقدم کا مؤخر میں دخول اس وقت لازم آتا جب ہم وضع کو اقتضاء صریحی میں داخل کرتے جبکہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ ہم اقتضاء سے اسکا ایسا عام معنی مراد لیتے ہیں کہ بیک وقت صریحی و ضمنی دونوں کا اطلاق اور صدق ہو سکتا ہے اور اس میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے کہ کوئی ایسا معنی ہو جو مقدم و مؤخر دونوں کو شامل ہو اور زیر بحث مسئلہ میں یہی صورت متحقق ہے۔ ہاں مضائقہ اس میں ہے کہ متقدم من حیث متقدم ، متأخر میں داخل ہو اور ہم اسکے قائل نہیں ۔
If it (the previous) is prior, then how can it be said that wad' is included in iqtida'? Because the prior is not included in the later. Answer: They say that the thing we are trying to establish, the objection of al-Tahrir is not harmful to us because iqtida' has two meanings: one specific iqtida' and the other general iqtida'. The general iqtida' encompasses both the sarihi (explicit) and dimni (implicit) types, whereas the specific iqtida', on the contrary, is only applied to explicit iqtida'. The iqtida' mentioned in the definition of hukm is the general iqtida' which includes sarihi and dimni (wad'). As for specific iqtida', which applies only to explicit, it is not intended. The summary of the answer is that the inclusion of the prior in the later would be necessary only if we included wad' in explicit iqtida'. That is not the case. Rather, we intend by iqtida' a general meaning that can apply to both explicit and implicit simultaneously, and there is no problem in that. There is a meaning that includes both prior and later, and this is realized in the issue under discussion. Yes, the problem would be if the prior qua prior were included in the later, but we do not hold that.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection against the definition of hukm. The objection: if wad' (position/status) is part of iqtida' (implication), then the definition would be circular because the defined (hukm) would include something prior. The author clarifies that iqtida' has two meanings: specific (only explicit text-based implication) and general (including both explicit and implicit, like wad'). The definition uses general iqtida', which encompasses wad', so no circularity occurs. The author emphasizes that they do not claim the prior as prior is included in the later, only that there is a common meaning covering both.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication; in this context, the relationship between the address of the Lawgiver and the ruling. Two types: specific (only explicit) and general (both explicit and implicit, including wad').
وضع (wad'): Positional/status rulings (ahkam wad'iyya) such as causality, condition, and preventer; considered by some as part of iqtida'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many meanings does iqtida' have in the context of the definition of hukm?
A) One meaning
B) Two meanings: specific and general
C) Three meanings
D) No specific meaning

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' in the Hanafi definition of hukm is general, encompassing both explicit and implicit implications, thereby including wad'i rulings without circularity.

Wad'i Rulings and the Definition of Hukm
Second answer to objection: wad'i rulings are not considered hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the exclusion of wad'i rulings from the definition of hukm is considered weak in Hanafi usul and identify the role of ancient terminology in this debate.

Core Idea
Excluding wad'i rulings from hukm is weak because ancient usage included them with no clear motive to change.

In this passage, we examine a second attempt to salvage the definition of hukm by claiming that wad'i rulings (causes, conditions, preventers) are not really part of 'hukm' as defined. The author, however, signals this answer is weak. Understanding why requires us to look at the historical evolution of terminology and the principle that a new technical term must have a clear basis to depart from the old one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and the second answer
The objection is that the definition of hukm (address of Allah related to acts) does not cover wad'i rulings. The second answer is that wad'i rulings are not part of the defined term 'hukm' at all.
The second answer tries to avoid the objection by redefining what is included in 'hukm'.
2
Analyze the strength of the answer
The author uses the term 'يمنع' (he prevents) in a tone of tamrid (weakness). The commentator notes that the answer is weak because the ancient terminology used 'hukm' to include wad' as well, and no motive for exclusion is apparent.
Tamrid is a linguistic tool to indicate a weak or contested opinion.
3
Understand the role of ancient vs. modern terminology
In usul, terms can evolve, but a change from ancient to modern usage requires a justifiable reason (dai'yah). Without such reason, the exclusion is arbitrary.
The commentator draws from al-Tahrir to emphasize this principle.
4
Assess the Hanafi tarjih
The weak answer is not relied upon; the stronger view is that wad'i rulings should be included or the definition should be adjusted.
The passage does not give the preferred view explicitly, but indicates the weakness of the exclusion.

قوله - وتارة يمنع كونها من المحدود فانا لانسمى حكما الخ. درج بالا عبارت میں حکم کی تعریف کے جامع نہ ہونے کے اعتراض کا دوسرا جواب ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ بعض اصولین نے کہا ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف پر احکام وصفیہ کے خروج کا اعتراض اس وقت پڑتا جب محدود (حکم) میں احکام وضعیہ داخل ہوتے اور جد اس کو شامل نہ ہوتا ، حالانکہ ایسا نہیں کیونکہ احکام وضعیہ محدود میں داخل ہی نہیں ہیں چونکہ احکام وضعیہ کو ہم در حقیقت حکم ہی نہیں مانتے گرچہ دوسروں نے اس کو حکم کے قبیل سے مانا ہے اور اصطلاح میں کوئی نزاع نہیں مصنف کے نزدیک یہ جواب نہ پا پسندیدہ ہے اس لئے اپنے قول و تارہ يمنع " صیغہ تمریض سے جواب کے ضعف کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔ تحریر نے اس مقام پر جواب کے ضعف کی طرف اشارہ کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ اصطلاح میں اگر چه اختلاف و نزاع نہیں ہوتا لیکن اس حقیقت سے انکار نہیں کیا جاسکتا کہ لفظ حکم کے وضع کے اعتبار سے اس میں مشاحت کی راہ ہے کیونکہ حکم اصطلاح قدیم میں وضع کے لئے بھی موضوع تھا اس لئے کہ اصطلاح قدیم وضع کو حکم میں داخل کرنے پر جازم و مصر تھا پس اصطلاح جدید کے لئے ایسا داعیہ چاہیئے جو اس کو اصطلاح قدیم سے خارج کرنے کا باعث بنے جب کہ یہاں اخراج کا کوئی باعث نظر نہیں آتا۔
His statement: 'And sometimes he prevents that they are from the defined, for we do not call it a ruling, etc.' In the above expression, this is the second answer to the objection that the definition of hukm is not comprehensive. It is that some usuli scholars have said that the objection that descriptive rulings (ahkam wasfiyya) are excluded from the definition of hukm would arise only if wad'i rulings (positional rulings) were included in the defined (hukm) and the definition did not include them. However, that is not the case because wad'i rulings are not included in the defined at all, since we do not really consider wad'i rulings to be hukm, even though others have considered them to be of the category of hukm. There is no dispute in terminology. According to the author (of Muslim al-Thubut), this answer is not favored, so he indicated the weakness of the answer by using the formula of tamrid (indicating weakness) in his statement 'يمنع'. Al-Tahrir says at this point, while pointing out the weakness of the answer, that although there is no dispute in terminology, it cannot be denied that the word 'hukm' in its original coinage (wad') allows for dispute, because in the ancient terminology, 'hukm' was applied to wad' as well. The ancient school was decisively of the view that wad' is included in hukm. Therefore, for the new terminology, there must be a motive to exclude it from the ancient usage, but here no motive for exclusion is apparent.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
The passage presents a second answer to the objection that the definition of hukm (as 'the address of Allah related to the acts of the obligated') does not include wad'i rulings (like causality, condition, etc.). Some usuli scholars argue that wad'i rulings are not part of the defined term 'hukm' at all, so the definition does not need to include them. The author (of Muslim al-Thubut) notes that this answer is weak. He uses the term 'يمنع' (he prevents) in a tone of tamrid (showing weakness). The commentator (Sharh) adds from al-Tahrir that although there is no formal disagreement in terminology, the ancient usage of 'hukm' did include wad'i rulings, so there should be a clear reason to exclude them in the new terminology. Since no such reason is given, the answer is considered weak.

Key Terms
احکام وضعیہ (ahkam wad'iyya): Positional/status rulings such as cause (سبب), condition (شرط), and preventer (مانع); rulings that establish a status rather than a direct obligation or prohibition.
محدود (mahdud): The defined term, i.e., 'hukm' in the definition being discussed.
تمریض (tamrid): A rhetorical device in which the author expresses a statement in a way that indicates its weakness or lack of preference, often by using verbs like 'يمنع' or 'قيل'.
داعیہ (dai'yah): A motive, cause, or justification that prompts a change in terminology or a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author (Muslim al-Thubut) consider the second answer regarding wad'i rulings to be weak?
A) Because wad'i rulings are clearly part of hukm
B) Because the ancient terminology included wad'i rulings in hukm, and there is no motive to exclude them
C) Because the definition is incomplete
D) Because the answer is not based on any proof

Key Takeaway
The exclusion of wad'i rulings from the definition of hukm is methodologically weak because it lacks a justified motive to depart from the ancient inclusive usage.

The Eternity of Hukm: Responding to the Mu'tazila Objection
Explaining Mu'tazila objection and Hanafi response on hukm's temporality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi view that hukm is eternal from the Mu'tazila view that it is temporal, and explain the role of ta'alluq in resolving the objection.

Core Idea
Hukm is eternal as an attribute; only its relation to actions is temporal.

This passage addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila against the Hanafi definition of hukm as divine speech (khitab). The Mu'tazila argue that since hukm is subject to abrogation (naskh) and temporal change, it cannot be an eternal attribute. The Hanafi response introduces a critical distinction: the essence of hukm is eternal, while its relation (ta'alluq) to created actions is temporal. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi theory of divine attributes and their relation to the temporal world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila claim that hukm is temporal because of naskh and because it can be characterized as becoming halal after haram. Understand their two arguments.
The objection is not just about naskh but also about the linguistic characterization of rulings as temporal.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi/Ash'ari position rejects the major premise that every hukm is temporal. They distinguish between the essence of hukm (eternal) and its ta'alluq (temporal).
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the nature of divine attributes.
3
Trace the proof structure
The argument: divine attributes are eternal; naskh changes only the relation, not the attribute itself. See how this preserves both eternity and applicability.
Key: the essence of hukm is an attribute, so it cannot be temporal.
4
Apply the distinction to the definition
Since both hukm (essence) and khitab are eternal, defining hukm as khitab is valid. The objection fails because it conflates essence with relation.
The definition remains sound; the Mu'tazila's inference from temporality is invalid.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: for Mu'tazila, rulings are created; for Ahl al-Haqq, rulings (as attributes) are uncreated. This affects theology and the nature of divine command.
This highlights the deeper theological stakes of the dispute.

اشرف النعوت شرح مسلم الثبوت ۳۱۴ حکم حدوث سے متصف ہوتا ہے چنانچہ کہا جاتا ہے کہ فلاں شئی حلال ہوگئی حرام ہونے کے بعد، پس حکم وہ حلال ہے جواب حادث ہوا ہے حالانکہ پہلے یہ حکم حلت نہ تھا اور جو حدوث کے ساتھ متصف ہوگا وہ قدیم نہیں ہو سکتا ہے لہذا احکم حادث ہے۔ علاوہ ازیں یہ بھی کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ حکم علت حادثہ کا معلل ہے اور ہر وہ شئی جو علت حادثہ کا معلل ہو حادث ہے۔ کبری ظاہر ہے رہے صغریٰ تو اسکا بیان یہ ہے کہا جاتا ہے۔ حلت المرأة بالنكاح وحرمت بالطلاق، یعنی عورت نکاح سے حلال ہوگئی اور طلاق سے حرام ہوگئی ، حلال و حرام ہونا حکم ہے جو بند کے فعل نکاح و طلاق کا معلل ہے اور اس میں کوئی خفا نہیں کہ بندہ اپنے جمیع افعال کے ساتھ حادث ہے حاصل یہ کہ حکم حرمت مرات وحلت مرآت کی علت ، طلاق و نکاح حادث ہے تو حکم بھی حادث ہے کیونکہ حادث کا معلل ہے اور جو حادث کا متلل ہے وہ حادث ہوتا ہے لہذ ا حکم حادث ہے۔ خلاصہ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب حکم کا حادث ہونا متعین ہے تو حکم کی تعریف خطاب ( کلام نفسی) سے کرنا صحیح نہیں کیونکہ خطاب کلام نفسی قدیم ہے پس اسوقت حادث کی تعریف قدیم سے کرنا لازم آئے گا اور یہ منع ہے ورنہ حادث کا قدیم اور قدیم کا حادث ہو نالا زم آئے گا و الکل باطل" جواب : کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ " معتزلہ کی دلیل کا کبری۔ یعنی معرف حکم ہے اور ہر حکم حادث ہے ممنوع ہے کہ حکم حادث نہیں ہے بلکہ خطاب کی طرح قدیم ہے رہے اس پر حدوث کا طاری ہونا تو یادر ہے کہ حدوث ، حکم پر نہیں طاری ہوتا بلکہ افعال عباد کے ساتھ جو حکم متعلق ہے اس حکم کے تعلق پر حدوث طاری ہوتا ہے اور تعلق کے حدوث سے متعلق کا حدوث لازم نہیں آتا ، چنانچہ اس حیثیت سے کہ تعلق حادث ہے حکم پر نسخ کا اطلاق جائز ہے کیونکہ نسخ حقیقت میں حکم کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہوتا بلکہ تعلق حکم کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ مختصر یہ کہ نفس حکم قطع نظر فعل عبد کے ساتھ تعلق کے پس وہ قدیم ہے اور یہی معرف ہے اور جب حکم بھی قدیم ہے اور خطاب بھی قدیم ہے تو اب حادث کی تعریف قدیم سے کر نالازم نہ آئے گا۔ حدوث حکم پر ذکر کردہ وجوہ کا بطلان :۔ رہے تم معتزلیوں کے ذکر کردہ وجوہ تو وہ سب فاسد ہیں اسلئے کہ تمہارا یہ کہنا کہ حکم میں نسخ ثابت ہے اور نسخ عدمی حادث ہے، اس سے مراد اگر
The second from the Mu'tazila is that the khitab (i.e., internal speech) according to you is eternal, and the hukm is temporal due to the establishment of naskh. But that which is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. The answer is that the temporal is the relation. So understand! Commentary: His statement 'the second from the Mu'tazila...' is the expression of the second discussion among the four proofs mentioned under the definition of hukm. In this discussion, the Mu'tazila object to the people of truth that defining hukm as khitab is not correct because according to you khitab is eternal and hukm is temporal. The details of the objection and answer are given below. Objection: The Mu'tazila's objection is that for you hukm is defined as 'the speech of Allah Most High related to the actions of the obligated', and khitab (internal speech) is eternal because it is not from the genus of letters and sounds or literal speech, but rather like other attributes such as hearing, sight, power, knowledge, etc., it is established with the Essence of Allah. And when khitab is the name of internal speech which is established with the Essence of Allah, then obviously its eternity is necessary and its non-existence is impossible, while the definition which is hukm is temporal because naskh is established for hukm, and naskh causes hukm to cease after its existence. In sum, non-existence occurs upon hukm, and that upon which non-existence occurs cannot be eternal, otherwise it would become eternal. Similarly, that whose eternity is established, its non-existence is impossible, otherwise the eternal would become temporal. So when non-existence of hukm is not prevented because non-existence occurs upon it due to naskh, then it is established that hukm is not eternal but temporal. Second exposition: Another exposition of the temporality of hukm can be that hukm is characterized by temporality. Thus it is said: such-and-such thing became halal after being haram. So hukm is the halal that occurred as temporal, while before this hukm of permissibility was not. And that which is characterized by temporality cannot be eternal. Hence hukm is temporal. Additionally, we can also say that hukm is the effect of a temporal cause, and everything that is the effect of a temporal cause is temporal. The major premise is obvious; as for the minor premise, its explanation is that it is said: 'the woman became permissible through marriage and became forbidden through divorce.' Being halal and being haram are rulings that are effects of the servant's actions of marriage and divorce. And there is no concealment that the servant with all his actions is temporal. In sum, the cause of the ruling of prohibition of women and permissibility of women, namely divorce and marriage, is temporal, so the ruling is also temporal because it is the effect of a temporal cause, and whatever is the effect of a temporal cause is temporal. Therefore, the ruling is temporal. Summary of objection: When the temporality of hukm is determined, then defining hukm as khitab (internal speech) is not correct because khitab (internal speech) is eternal. So then defining the temporal by the eternal would be necessary, which is prevented, otherwise it would necessitate the temporal becoming eternal and the eternal becoming temporal, all of which is false. Answer: The summary of the answer is that the major premise of the Mu'tazila's proof—i.e., the definition is hukm and every hukm is temporal—is rejected. Hukm is not temporal but rather eternal like khitab. As for temporality occurring upon it, understand that temporality does not occur upon the hukm itself but rather upon the relation of the hukm to the actions of the servants. And from the temporality of the relation, the temporality of the related does not necessarily follow. Thus, in the sense that the relation is temporal, applying naskh to the hukm is permissible because naskh in reality does not relate to the hukm itself but rather relates to the relation of the hukm. In short, the essence of the hukm, regardless of its relation to the servant's action, is eternal, and this is the definition. And when both hukm and khitab are eternal, then defining the temporal by the eternal does not become necessary. Nullification of the mentioned reasons for temporality of hukm: As for the reasons you Mu'tazila mentioned, all are invalid because your statement that naskh is established in hukm and naskh is a temporal non-existence, if by that you mean...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of a theological-legal dispute between the Mu'tazila and the Ahl al-Haqq (specifically the Ash'ari/Maturidi position adopted by Hanafi usul scholars) regarding the definition of hukm (ruling). The Mu'tazila object that if hukm is defined as 'khitab Allah' (divine speech), and khitab is eternal (qadim) because it is an attribute of Allah, then hukm cannot be temporal (hadith). However, they argue that hukm is temporal due to naskh (abrogation) and other reasons. The Hanafi response, as explained by the commentator, distinguishes between the essence of hukm (which is an eternal attribute) and its ta'alluq (relation) to the actions of servants. Naskh only changes the relation, not the ruling itself. This preserves both the eternity of divine speech and the applicability of rulings to temporal actions. The text first presents the Mu'tazila objection in two formulations: one based on naskh and another based on the characterization of hukm as temporal (e.g., something becoming halal after being haram). Then it provides the definitive answer: the ruling itself is eternal; only its relation is temporal. Thus, defining hukm as khitab is valid because both are eternal. The commentator also refutes the Mu'tazila's reasons for temporality by asserting that they misunderstand naskh and causality.

Key Terms
خطاب (khitab): Here it means internal speech (kalam nafsi), an eternal attribute of Allah.
حكم (hukm): The ruling from Allah related to the actions of the obligated. In this context, the essence of the ruling is eternal.
تعلق (ta'alluq): The relation of the eternal ruling to the temporal actions of the servant. This relation is temporal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is temporal about a hukm?
A) The essence of the hukm itself
B) The naskh that abrogates the hukm
C) The relation (ta'alluq) of the hukm to actions
D) The definition of hukm

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between eternal hukm and temporal ta'alluq resolves the apparent conflict between the eternity of divine speech and the temporality of legal changes.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
The Eternity of Divine Ruling and Its Temporal Relation
Defending the eternity of divine ruling against objections based on temporal relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the eternal ruling (hukm qadim) and its temporal relation (taluq), and analyze the Hanafi qiyas from the Quranic analogy to defend the eternity of the ruling.

Core Idea
The divine ruling is eternal; only its application to events is temporal — like the eternal Quranic speech expressed in temporal recitation.

This passage addresses a key objection in usul al-fiqh: if divine rulings relate to temporal events, how can they be eternal? The Hanafi response distinguishes between the ruling itself (qadim) and its relation (taluq) to events (hadith). By drawing an analogy with the Quran — temporal recitation pointing to eternal divine speech — the author defends the immutability of God's rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Claim
The opponent argues that the ruling (hukm) must be temporal because it pertains to temporal events. Locate this claim in the passage.
2
Distinguish Ruling from Relation
The Hanafi response separates the eternal ruling itself from its temporal relation (taluq). Only the relation is temporal.
The key phrase: 'its relation is qualified by temporality, not the ruling itself.'
3
Apply the Quranic Analogy
The author uses the analogy of the Quran: recited words are temporal, but they indicate eternal divine speech (kalam nafsi). Similarly, temporal acts indicate eternal rulings.
This is a qiyas: the asl is the Quran; the far' is the ruling; the illa is the relation between temporal manifestation and eternal essence.
4
Refute the Objection
The opponent's argument that a new ruling after non-existence implies temporality is refuted: only the relation is new, not the ruling.
Example: 'a thing becomes permissible after it was not' means permissibility became related to the thing, not that the ruling itself changed.
5
State the Hanafi Conclusion
The divine ruling remains eternal and unchanging. Its manifestation in temporal events does not affect its essence.
This position underpins the Hanafi view that God's attributes are eternal.

یہ ہے کہ جو حکم از لی قدیم ہے وہ عدمی حادث ہے تو یہ ممنوع ہے اور اگر اس سے حکم از لی قدیم سے تعلق کا عدمی حادث ہونا مراد ہے تو مسلم ہے لیکن اس سے تمہارا مطلوب حدوث حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا ۔ ایسے ہی یہ کہنا کہ حکم حدوث سے متصف ہوتا ہے ممنوع ہے اس لئے کہ حادث سے کم نہیں بلکہ اسکا تعلق متصف ہوتا ہے پس زیادہ سے زیادہ تعلق حکم کا حدوث سے اتصاف لازم آئے گا حکم کا نہیں چونکہ ہمارے قول "حل الشئی بعد مالم محل (شئی نا جائز ہونے کے بعد حلال ہوئی ) کا معنی اند تعلق الحل بعد مالم یکن متعلقا ( یعنی شئی سے حلت متعلق ہوئی بعد اس کے کہ اس سے پہلے اس کے ساتھ حلت متعلق نہیں تھی) ہے۔ اسی طرح تمہاری تیسری وجہ کا فساد بھی ظاہر ہے چنانچہ ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ حکم فعل حادث کا معلل ہے نیز وہ فعل حادث اس میں مؤثر بھی ہے یہاں تک کہ لازم آئے کہ حادث ، حکم قدیم کے منافی ہے چونکہ علت حادثہ سے مراد مبین ہے یعنی بندہ کا فعل حادث حکم کا مظہر و بین ہے اور اس میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں کہ فعل حادث قدیم کا مظہر و مبین ہو۔ دیکھو مصحف شریف میں مخصوص نقوش والا کلام ، قاری قرآن کے منھ سے مخصوص الفاظ وحروف اور مختلف انداز و بیان سے نکلا ہوا کلام حادث ہے مگر یہی کلام لفظی حادث ہوتے ہوئے کلام نفسی قدیم پر دال ہے۔
This is that: if what is meant is that a ruling that is eternal in itself is a non-existent temporal, then this is rejected; and if what is meant is that the relation of an eternal ruling is a non-existent temporal, then it is conceded, but your desired outcome of the temporality of the ruling is not proven. Similarly, saying that the ruling is qualified by temporality is rejected because it is not the ruling that is temporal, but rather its relation is qualified. Thus, at most, the relation of the ruling being qualified by temporality is entailed, not the ruling itself. Since our statement 'hal ash-shay' ba'da ma lam yahill' (a thing becomes permissible after it was not) means 'talliq al-hill ba'da ma lam yakun muta'alliqan' (i.e., permissibility became related to the thing after it was not related to it before). Similarly, the corruption of your third proof is also apparent. For we do not accept that the ruling is caused by a temporal act, nor that the temporal act is effective in it, to the extent that it would entail that a temporal is opposite to an eternal ruling. Since what is meant by a temporal cause is a manifestor (mubayyin), i.e., the temporal act of the servant is a manifestation and indicator of the ruling, and there is no harm in a temporal act being a manifestation of an eternal thing. Look at the specific script of the Mushaf—the words uttered by the Quran reciter, consisting of particular letters and expressions, and emerging in various styles, are temporal, yet this temporal verbal speech indicates the eternal inner speech (kalam nafsi).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a typical Hanafi usul argument defending the eternity of the divine ruling (hukm) against objections that it appears to be temporal because it relates to contingent events. The author distinguishes between the ruling itself, which is eternal (qadim) and unchanging, and its relation (taluq) to events, which is temporal. The analogy of the Quran is used: the recited words are temporal, but they point to the eternal attribute of speech in God. The opponent's argument that the ruling must be temporal because it 'occurs' after a state of non-existence is refuted by showing that only the relation to the event is new, not the ruling itself. This is a foundational Hanafi position that rejects the idea that God's rulings change over time.

Key Terms
حکم (hukm): The divine ruling that is eternal and unchanging in itself, but its application to events occurs in time.
تعلق (taluq): The connection or relation between the eternal ruling and temporal actions; this relation is temporal.
قدیم (qadim): Eternal, without beginning; used to describe the divine ruling.
کلام نفسی (kalam nafsi): Inner speech, the eternal attribute of Allah's speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct interpretation of the phrase 'a thing becomes permissible after it was not'?
A) The ruling of permissibility came into existence at that moment.
B) The relation of permissibility to the thing became new.
C) The object itself changed from impermissible to permissible.
D) The ruling changed because of a new cause.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that the divine ruling is eternal (qadim) and only its relation (taluq) to events is temporal is defended by the analogy of the Quran: temporal recitation indicates eternal speech.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
8 min
Objection to the Definition of Hukm: Do Minors' Actions Invalidate It?
Objection to definition of hukm, Shafi'i answer, Hanafi critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection to the Hanafi definition of hukm based on minors' actions, explain the two responses, and evaluate the Hanafi critique using the principle that thawab does not require taklif.

Core Idea
Minors' actions carry legal effects but not because they are mukallaf—the definition of hukm stands.

The definition of hukm as 'the address of the Lawgiver concerning the actions of the mukallafin' seems to exclude minors, who are not mukallaf, yet their actions (prayer, sale, liability) attract rulings like mandub, sahih, and wajib. This objection, if unanswered, would force a redefinition. In this lesson, we walk through the objection, a Shafi'i-style answer, and the Hanafi critique that preserves the definition by distinguishing legal address from divine reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first paragraph: the definition of hukm is said to be نقض (contradicted) by the actions of a minor. List the three examples given (prayer being mandub, sale being sahih, liability being wajib). Note that these are genuine ahkam, yet the definition excludes them because of the condition 'mukallaf'.
The objection assumes that if an action has a hukm, it must fall under the definition of hukm.
2
Analyze the first answer (Shafi'i books)
The answer states that the address (khitab) is not to the minor but to the guardian (wali). The hadith 'Command your children to pray' is cited as evidence. Understand the logic: the guardian is encouraged, the minor is not directly addressed. For validity of sale, it is claimed to be a rational matter, not a Sharia ruling.
This answer tries to remove minors' actions from the scope of hukm proper.
3
Critique the first answer
The commentator says 'fihi ma fihi' (there is something in it). The hashiah points out that (1) the minor's prayer is rewarded, which fits the definition of mandub; (2) if mandub depends on the guardian, an orphan with no guardian would have his prayer be futile, contradicting Quranic promise. Therefore, the minor must be directly eligible for reward.
The critique shows that reward (thawab) attaches to the minor's act, so it does have a hukm-like quality.
4
Locate the Hanafi resolution
The text then clarifies that thawab is not dependent on taklif. Allah's sunna is to reward good deeds even from non-mukallaf. So the minor's prayer is rewarded but is not technically 'mandub' because that requires a taklifi address. The definition of hukm remains intact: it only covers actions of the mukallaf. The minor's actions fall under divine bounty, not the legal address.
This preserves the definition while acknowledging that minors can receive reward.
5
Apply the usul principle
This passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that taklif is a condition for the five taklifi rulings (wajib, haram, etc.). Thawab is a separate category of divine grace. In istinbat, we distinguish between direct legal address (khitab al-taklif) and indirect effects (thawab). The hadith 'Command your children' is directed to guardians, not minors, supporting the Hanafi definition.
This is a key usul distinction: ruling (hukm) vs. reward (thawab).
