یہ عبارت مندرجہ بالا عبارت ویشار کا نعمانی استحقاق العقاب بالترک پر متفرع ہے معنی یہ ہے کہ جب ایسا ہے کہ ایجاب و کراہت تحریم ، افتراض و تحریم کے ساتھ استحقاق عذاب میں مشترک ہیں تو اسی وجہ سے حضرت امام محمد رحمۃ اللہ علیہ نے ہر مکروہ کو حرام مجازاً کہا ہے یعنی مکروہ پر حرام کا اطلاق مجازی طور پر ہے حقیقی نہیں۔ چنانچہ امام محمد وجوب و مکروہ کے منکر کی تکفیر نہیں کرتے جیسا کہ آپ فرض وحرام کے منکر کی تکفیر کرتے ہیں پس اگر ان کے نزدیک مکروہ پر حرام کا اطلاق معنی حقیقی پر ہوتا تو مکروہ کے منکر پر کفر کا قبول کرتے جیسا کہ منکر حرام کی تکفیر کرتے ہیں جس کا نتیجہ یہ نکلتا ہے کہ امام محمد کے نزدیک مکروہ پر حرام کا اطلاق مجازی ہے اس مناسبت سے کہ مکروہ میں حرام کی طرح ترک کف کے سب
From Ashraf al-Nu'ut Sharh Muslim al-Thubut. Page 326: also if the prohibited [act] is not preponderant, then that ruling is makruh. The summary is that according to the restriction of requirement, there are four types of ruling: ijab, tahrim, nadb, and karaha. As for the restriction of choice, there is only one type, which is where action and refraining are equal, that is ibaha. Thus the ruling itself becomes five types. His statement: 'And the Hanafis looked at the state of the indicator (dal) and said: if the demand is established...' The division of rulings according to Hanafi taste: This statement divides rulings according to Hanafi methodology. Note that the previous division of rulings considered the real ruling itself, i.e., the addressed meaning (madlul) that the verbal address indicates. But when the Hanafis saw that rulings established by categorical evidence are different from those established by probable evidence, and that categorical evidence gives categorical indication and probable evidence gives probable indication, they considered the state of the indicator (the spoken words) rather than the state of the indicated. Therefore, this division is actually of the indicator, not of the ruling (indicated). However, the division of the indicator also results in the division of the ruling. So they divided rulings by considering the state of the indicator and said: 'If the demand is categorical...' That is, if a categorical demand is established by categorical evidence in which there is no doubt at all, then this ruling is iftiraad if the demand concerns action, and it is the ruling of tahrim if the categorical demand concerns refraining. If a categorical demand is established by probable evidence in which there is doubt, then this is ijab if the demand concerns action, and the ruling is karaha tahrimiyya if the demand concerns refraining. If the demand is not categorical but preponderant, and it concerns action not refraining, then it is mandub, and if it concerns refraining, then it is karaha tanzihiyya. And if there is no demand at all but action and refraining are equal, then it is ibaha. Thus the ruling has seven types: iftiraad, ijab, tahrim, karaha tanzihiyya, karaha tahrimiyya, mandub, and ibaha. Proof of the enumeration: The ruling either involves a demand or not; if the latter, it is ibaha; if the former, either the demand is categorical or preponderant. If preponderant, it relates to action or refraining; if to action, it is nadb; if to refraining, it is karaha tanzihiyya. If the demand is categorical, it is established by categorical evidence or by probable evidence. In the second case, if the demand concerns action, it is ijab; if refraining, it is karaha tahrimiyya. In the first case, if established by categorical evidence, if the demand concerns action it is iftiraad; if refraining it is tahrim. His statement: 'And he indicates that they are alike in deserving punishment for leaving.' With the above statement, the author wants to explain that according to the Hanafis, ijab and karaha tahrimiyya, although different from iftiraad and tahrim and opposite to them, are partners with them in some rulings. So just as one deserves punishment for leaving fard, similarly one deserves punishment for leaving wajib. Likewise, just as one deserves punishment for committing haram, similarly one deserves punishment for committing makruh tahrimi. Because the leaving of a wajib act shares with the leaving of a fard act in deserving punishment, i.e., there is no difference between fard and wajib, and between makruh tahrimi and haram regarding deserving punishment for leaving the act or refraining. Rather, they are equal. Therefore, the one who leaves wajib is like the one who leaves fard, and the one who commits makruh tahrimi is like the one who commits haram in deserving punishment. His statement: 'And from this, Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said...' This statement is derived from the above statement about deserving punishment for leaving. The meaning is that since ijab and karaha tahrimiyya share with iftiraad and tahrim in deserving punishment, for this reason Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) metaphorically called every makruh as haram, i.e., applying the term haram to makruh is metaphorical, not real. Thus, Imam Muhammad does not declare the denier of wajib and makruh to be an unbeliever, as he declares the denier of fard and haram to be an unbeliever. So if, in his view, the application of the term haram to makruh were real, then he would have declared the denier of makruh to be a disbeliever, just as he declares the denier of haram to be a disbeliever. The conclusion is that in Imam Muhammad's view, applying the term haram to makruh is metaphorical, due to the similarity that makruh involves refraining from an act just like haram.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the Hanafi classification of legal rulings (ahkam) based on the strength of the evidence (dalil). The author first notes that according to the aspect of 'requirement' (iqtida), there are four categories: ijab (requiring action), tahrim (requiring refraining), nadb (recommending action), and karaha (recommending refraining). With the aspect of choice (takhyir), there is one category: ibaha (permissibility). This yields five types when looking only at the ruling itself. However, the Hanafis introduced a further distinction by considering the evidence that indicates the ruling. They observed that rulings established by categorical evidence (qat'i) are different from those established by probable evidence (zanni). Therefore, they divided rulings into seven types: fard (required action with categorical evidence), wajib (required action with probable evidence), haram (required refraining with categorical evidence), makruh tahrimi (required refraining with probable evidence), mandub (recommended action), makruh tanzihi (recommended refraining), and mubah (neutral). The passage then provides a logical proof for this sevenfold division: first, a ruling either involves a demand or not; if no demand, it's mubah. If there is a demand, it is either categorical or preponderant. If preponderant, it either demands action (mandub) or refraining (makruh tanzihi). If categorical, it is either from categorical evidence (then fard if action, haram if refraining) or from probable evidence (then wajib if action, makruh tahrimi if refraining). The author then clarifies that despite the difference in evidence strength, fard and wajib share the consequence of deserving punishment for leaving, and haram and makruh tahrimi share deserving punishment for committing. This is why Imam Muhammad metaphorically called makruh tahrimi 'haram'. However, he did not declare the denier of wajib or makruh tahrimi to be an unbeliever, unlike the denier of fard or haram, because the metaphorical usage does not imply disbelief. This passage is crucial for understanding the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib, which has significant implications for jurisprudence.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): A categorical demand (talib jazim) for action established by categorical evidence (dalil qat'i).
واجب (wajib): A categorical demand for action established by probable evidence (dalil zanni).
حرام (haram): A categorical demand for refraining (kaf) established by categorical evidence.
کراہت تحریمی (karaha tahrimiyya): A categorical demand for refraining established by probable evidence.
ندب (nadb): A preponderant (rajih) demand for action, not categorical.
اباحت (ibaha): No demand; action and refraining are equal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the seven types of rulings according to Hanafi usul?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Makruh Tahrimi
D) Sunnah Mu'akkadah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies rulings into seven types by considering both the nature of the demand and the strength of the evidence, with fard/wajib and haram/makruh tahrimi distinguished by evidence strength (qat'i vs. zanni).

Fard vs Wajib and Haram vs Makruh Tahrimi: Evidence Strength and Shared Punishment
Explains shared punishment and terminological nuance between fard/wajib and haram/makruh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fard from wajib and haram from makruh tahrimi based on evidentiary strength (qat'i vs zanni), and explain why the intra-Hanafi disagreement over naming is purely verbal.

Core Idea
Fard and haram are proven by qat'i evidence; wajib and makruh tahrimi by zanni evidence, yet they share liability for punishment.

In Hanafi usul, the categories of obligation (fard/wajib) and prohibition (haram/makruh tahrimi) are distinguished by the strength of their evidence, not by their legal consequence of punishment. This passage clarifies that while fard and haram are based on definitive proofs, wajib and makruh tahrimi rest on speculative proofs, yet all four incur punishment for non-compliance. The text also resolves a surface-level dispute among the early Hanafi imams: is makruh tahrimi truly 'haram'? The answer lies in understanding metaphor versus literal usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared legal consequence
The passage states that just as abandoning a fard deserves punishment, so does abandoning a wajib; similarly, committing a makruh tahrimi deserves punishment like committing a haram. Note the shared element: 'استحقاق العقاب' (liability for punishment).
2
Pinpoint the evidentiary basis for the distinction
The text explicitly links fard and haram to 'دلیل قطعی' (qat'i evidence) and wajib and makruh tahrimi to 'دلیل ظنی' (zanni evidence). This is the defining criterion that separates the two pairs, despite shared punishment.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school; other madhhabs often treat fard and wajib as synonyms.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute over terminology
Imam Muhammad called makruh tahrimi 'haram' metaphorically, because of the shared punishment. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) insisted on the literal term 'closer to haram' (أقرب إلى الحرام) to preserve the evidentiary difference.
The author emphasizes the dispute is verbal (لفظي), not substantive.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Imam Muhammad does not declare takfir on the denier of a wajib or makruh tahrimi, just as the Shaykhayn avoid applying the term 'haram' directly to prevent confusion. The difference in terminology does not affect the ruling on punishment.
Takfir is reserved for denying something established by qat'i evidence (fard/haram).
5
Apply the distinction to new cases
Given a specific act, determine its evidentiary strength (qat'i or zanni) and then classify it as fard/wajib or haram/makruh tahrimi. Verify both the punishment and the takfir consequence.
For example, the obligation of the five daily prayers is fard (qat'i), while the obligation of witr prayer is wajib (zanni).

الى الحرام اس لئے کہا ہے کہ مکروہ وہ فعل ہے جس کا ثبوت دلیل ظنی سے ہو اور حرام وہ ہے جسکا ثبوت دلیل قطعی سے ہو۔ اور دونوں میں فرق ظاہر ہے کہ ظنی سے ثابت ہونے والا قطعی سے ثابت ہونے والے کے منزل میں نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے مکروہ بعینہ حرام نہیں ہوسکتا البتہ حرام سے قریب تر ہو سکتا ہے۔ ماتن نے تجوزا اور والحقیقہ کے لفظ سے اشارہ کیا ہے کہ مکروہ پر حرام کے اطلاق میں فقہاء احناف کا اختلاف لفظی ہے حقیقی Commentary on the saying 'and they share in deserving punishment for omission.' From the above expression, it is intended to show that according to the Hanafis, ijab (obligation) and karaha tahrimiyya (prohibitory dislike), although they are different and opposite to iftiraḍ (making fard) and tahrim (prohibition) — such that each is the counter-part of the other — yet both of them share with the other two in some rulings. Thus, just as by abandoning a fard a person deserves punishment, likewise by abandoning a wajib he deserves punishment. Similarly, just as by committing a haram a person deserves punishment, likewise by committing a makruh tahrimi he deserves punishment. Because ijab of abandoning a wajib shares with iftiraḍ of abandoning a fard in deserving punishment, meaning there is no difference between fard and wajib, and between makruh tahrimi and haram, regarding the deserving of punishment due to abandoning an act or abandoning the opposite of an act; rather both are shared. Therefore, the abandoner of a wajib is like the abandoner of a fard, and the committer of a makruh tahrimi is like the committer of a haram in deserving punishment. The saying 'And from here Muhammad [ibn al-Hasan] said, may Allah have mercy on him, etc.' This sentence branches off from the aforementioned statement indicating the sharing in deserving punishment for abandonment. The meaning is that since it is the case that ijab and karaha tahrimi share with iftiraḍ and tahrim in deserving punishment, therefore Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, called every makruh 'haram' metaphorically, meaning the application of 'haram' to makruh is metaphorical, not literal. Accordingly, Imam Muhammad does not declare takfir (unbelief) of the denier of wajib and makruh, just as he declares takfir of the denier of fard and haram. For if in his view the application of 'haram' to makruh were literal, he would have affirmed kufr (unbelief) for the denier of makruh as he does for the denier of haram. The outcome is that according to Imam Muhammad, the application of 'haram' to makruh is metaphorical, due to the correspondence that in makruh there is deserving of punishment for refraining from the opposite, just as in haram. In contrast, the two shaykhs — Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf — stated on a literal basis that makruh tahrimi is not exactly haram, but rather makruh tahrimi is closer to prohibition than to permissibility. Because of the sharing in deserving punishment, the two shaykhs did not call makruh 'haram' but said 'closer to haram', because makruh is an act established by a zanni (speculative) evidence, whereas haram is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence. The difference is clear: what is proven by zanni evidence cannot be in the rank of what is proven by qat'i evidence, so makruh cannot be exactly haram, but it can be closer to haram. The author (al-matn) indicated by the words 'metaphorically' and 'literally' that the difference among Hanafi jurists in applying 'haram' to makruh is verbal, not real, because Imam Muhammad called makruh 'haram' metaphorically, and the two shaykhs called makruh 'closer to haram' literally. So understand this difference.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the distinction between fard, wajib, haram, and makruh tahrimi in Hanafi usul. The author first explains that although fard and wajib are distinct categories, they share the characteristic that abandoning either one deserves punishment. Similarly, haram and makruh tahrimi share the characteristic that committing them deserves punishment. The basis for this is that fard and haram are proven by qat'i (definitive) evidence, while wajib and makruh tahrimi are proven by zanni (speculative) evidence. Despite the difference in the strength of evidence, the legal consequence (punishment for non-compliance) is the same. Then the passage discusses a terminological dispute among Hanafi imams. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani sometimes called makruh tahrimi 'haram' but only metaphorically, because although makruh shares with haram the liability for punishment, it is not exactly the same due to the weaker evidence. Therefore, he does not declare takfir on someone who denies the obligation of a wajib or the prohibition of a makruh, whereas he would for denying a fard or haram. In contrast, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn) insisted on the literal distinction: they called makruh 'closer to haram' (aqrab ila al-haram) to reflect that it is not exactly haram but shares its punishment aspect. The author concludes that the difference is merely verbal, not substantive. This passage teaches students the nuanced hierarchy of legal categories in Hanafi usul, emphasizing that the strength of evidence (qat'i vs zanni) affects the category but not necessarily the worldly punishment, though it does affect the gravity of denial in terms of faith.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): A command established by qat'i evidence; its denial constitutes kufr and its abandonment deserves punishment.
الواجب (al-wajib): A command established by zanni evidence; its abandonment deserves punishment but its denial does not lead to kufr.
الحرام (al-haram): A prohibition established by qat'i evidence; its commission deserves punishment and its denial leads to kufr.
المکروہ التحریمی (al-makruh al-tahrimi): A prohibition established by zanni evidence; its commission deserves punishment but its denial does not lead to kufr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Fard is established by qat'i evidence, wajib by zanni evidence.
C) Both are established by qat'i evidence, but fard is more severe.
D) Fard is obligatory only in worship, wajib in all areas.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental distinction in Hanafi usul between fard/wajib and haram/makruh tahrimi is the strength of evidence (qat'i vs zanni), not the liability for punishment, which is shared; and the intra-madhhab terminological dispute is purely verbal.

Ijab vs Wujub: The Same Obligation, Two Perspectives
Clarifies the terminological distinction between two sets of hukm divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ijab and wujub (and tahrim and hurma) by correctly attributing each term to its proper locus (Lawgiver or act), and explain why they refer to the same legal reality from different perspectives.

Core Idea
Ijab and wujub are the same obligation — one seen from the Ruler, the other from the act.

This lesson clarifies a subtle but crucial point in Hanafi usul terminology. The same legal ruling can be described two ways: as an act of the Lawgiver (ijab/tahrim) or as a quality of the act (wujub/hurma). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when reading usul texts and helps you navigate apparent contradictions in the ulama's statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Pairs of Terms
Locate the terms ijab/tahrim and wujub/hurma in the passage. Note that they are used seemingly interchangeably by usul scholars.
The matn explicitly says 'they made the divisions of hukm once as ijab and tahrim, and another as wujub and hurma.'
2
Determine the Locus of Attribution
Understand that the difference lies in attribution: ijab/tahrim attribute the ruling to the Lawgiver (al-hakim), while wujub/hurma attribute it to the act itself.
The passage states: 'if’al when attributed to the Ruler is called ijab, when attributed to the act is called wujub.'
3
Work Through the Example
Take the command 'do!' (if’al). When considering Allah as the Commander, we say 'ijab' (He imposed an obligation). When considering the act of prayer itself, we say 'wujub' (prayer is obligatory).
This is the core illustration: same command, different perspectives.
4
Recognize the Unity of Reality
Understand that the two sets of terms are not referring to different rulings but to the same legal reality — just viewed from the cause (Lawgiver) or the effect (act).
The passage mentions that some scholars see it as leniency, but the preferred view (as per the matn) is that they are 'identical in essence, different in perspective'.
5
Apply the Distinction When Reading Usul Texts
When you encounter ijab and wujub in usul works, recognize that the author is simply choosing a perspective. No contradiction exists.
This resolves apparent inconsistencies in usul classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المسلم : "واعلم انهم جعلوا اقسام الحكم مرة الايجاب والتحريم واخرى الوجوب والحرمة فحمل بعضهم على المسامحة وبعضهم على انهما متحدان بالذات ومختلفان بالاعتبار فان معنى افعل اذانسب الى الحاكم سمى ايجاباً واذا نسب الى الفعل سمى وجوباً" ترجمہ : اور جان لو کہ اصولین نے حکم کی قسموں کو بھی ایجاب و تحریم کا نام دیا ہے اور بھی و جوب و حرمت کا پس بعض علماء نے اصولیین کے کلام کو مسامحت پر محمول کیا ہے اور بعض نے اس پر کہ وجوب وحرمت اور ایجاب و تحریم متحد بالذات ہیں اور مختلف بالاعتبار ہیں کیونکہ معنی افعل جب حاکم کی طرف منسوب ہو تو اسے ایجاب کہتے ہیں اور جب فعل کی طرف منسوب ہو تو اسے وجوب کہتے ہیں ۔ تشریح قوله واعلم انهم جعلوا اقسام الحكم مرة الايجاب والخ مندرجہ بالا عبارت سے ماتن نے اصولیین کے کلام میں تاقع وتزاحم دکھایا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ حضرات اصولین حکم کی قسموں کو کبھی ایجاب و تحریم کا نام دیتے ہیں جیسا کہ انقسام حکم کے مقام میں ابھی گذرا۔ اور کبھی حکم کی قسموں کو وجوب و حرمت سے تعبیر کرتے ہیں جیسا کہ فقہ کی تعریف "العلم بالا حکام"
The matn of Muslim al-Thubut: 'And know that they made the divisions of hukm once as ijab and tahrim, and another as wujub and hurma. Some scholars interpret their words as leniency, and others interpret that they are identical in essence and different in perspective, because the meaning of "do" (if'al) when attributed to the Ruler (al-hakim) is called ijab, and when attributed to the act is called wujub.' Translation (Urdu): 'And know that the usuliyun have called the divisions of hukm also ijab and tahrim, and also wujub and hurma. So some scholars interpret the words of the usuliyun as leniency, and others interpret that wujub/hurma and ijab/tahrim are identical in essence and different in perspective, because the meaning of 'do' when attributed to the ruler is called ijab, and when attributed to the act is called wujub.' Commentary: 'Explanation of the saying "and know that they made the divisions of hukm once as ijab and...": From the above expression, the author (al-matn) has shown the apparent contradiction and inconsistency in the words of the usuliyun, and said that the usuliyun sometimes call the divisions of hukm as ijab and tahrim, as has just passed in the section on the division of hukm, and sometimes express the divisions of hukm as wujub and hurma, as in the definition of fiqh: "knowledge of the rulings..."
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle point in usul terminology. The usuliyun (scholars of usul) sometimes classify legal rulings as ijab (obligation from the Lawgiver's perspective) and tahrim (prohibition), and other times as wujub (obligation from the act's perspective) and hurma (prohibition from the act's perspective). Some scholars see this as mere looseness (musamaha), while others argue that the two sets of terms refer to the same reality but from different angles: ijab/tahrim attribute the ruling to the Lawgiver (al-hakim), whereas wujub/hurma attribute it to the act itself. For example, the command 'do!' (if'al) when linked to the Lawgiver is called ijab, but when linked to the act it is called wujub. Thus there is no real contradiction; it is a matter of perspective. The commentary explains that the matn author points out this apparent inconsistency in the usuliyun's statements to clarify that there is no real conflict.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): Attribution of the command to the Lawgiver; the act of imposing an obligation from the Ruler's perspective.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The quality of being obligatory as it attaches to the act; the act's nature as due or incumbent.
التحريم (al-tahrim): Attribution of the prohibition to the Lawgiver; the act of prohibiting from the Ruler's perspective.
الحرمة (al-hurma): The quality of being prohibited as it attaches to the act; the act's nature as forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the command 'if’al' and the terms ijab and wujub?
A) 'If’al' is a different type of command from those that produce ijab or wujub.
B) When 'if’al' is attributed to the Lawgiver, it is called ijab; when attributed to the act, it is called wujub.
C) 'If’al' produces ijab but not wujub.
D) 'If’al' is irrelevant to the distinction.

Key Takeaway
Ijab/tahrim and wujub/hurma are the same legal reality seen from the Lawgiver’s perspective (cause) and the act’s perspective (effect).

Twofold Division of Hukm: Ijab/Tahrim and Wujub/Hurma
Explains the twofold classification of hukm and reconciles apparent contradictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ijab/tahrim and wujub/hurma and explain the Hanafi reconciliation of the twofold division.

Core Idea
Ijab and wujub are identical in essence; they differ only in attribution to the Lawgiver or the act.

This passage from al-Muslim al-Thubut addresses an apparent contradiction in usul terminology: scholars sometimes divide hukm into ijab/tahrim (command/prohibition) and sometimes into wujub/hurma (obligation/prohibition in the act). You will learn two approaches to reconcile this, clarifying the Hanafi position that these terms are either interpreted figuratively or seen as identical in essence but distinct in consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the twofold division
Recognise that hukm is divided into two sets of terms: (1) ijab/tahrim, and (2) wujub/hurma. Note that the first set refers to the act of the Lawgiver, the second to the quality of the act.
2
Locate the apparent contradiction
Observe that using two different sets for the same hukm seems inconsistent. The matn explicitly points this out as a conflict (tazahum).
3
Examine the first reconciliation (musamaha)
Understand that some scholars carry the speech on leniency: either the term 'hukm' (maqsim) is used metaphorically for wujub/hurma, or the effect (wujub/hurma) is used to mean the cause (ijab/tahrim).
This involves ta'wil (figurative interpretation) in either the divided thing or the division.
4
Examine the second reconciliation (identity in essence)
Grasp that ijab/tahrim and wujub/hurma are identical in essence (mutahidan bi al-dhat) but differ in consideration: ijab is obligation attributed to the ruler, wujub is obligation attributed to the act.
This is the preferred Hanafi view, as it avoids figurative language.

متن المسلم : "واعلم انهم جعلوا اقسام الحكم مرة الايجاب والتحريم واخرى الوجوب والحرمة فحمل بعضهم على المسامحة وبعضهم على انهما متحدان بالذات ومختلفان بالاعتبار فان معنى افعل اذانسب الى الحاكم سمى ايجاباً واذا نسب الى الفعل سمى وجوباً"
ترجمہ : اور جان لو کہ اصولین نے حکم کی قسموں کو بھی ایجاب و تحریم کا نام دیا ہے اور بھی و جوب و حرمت کا پس بعض علماء نے اصولیین کے کلام کو مسامحت پر محمول کیا ہے اور بعض نے اس پر کہ وجوب وحرمت اور ایجاب و تحریم متحد بالذات ہیں اور مختلف بالاعتبار ہیں کیونکہ معنی افعل جب حاکم کی طرف منسوب ہو تو اسے ایجاب کہتے ہیں اور جب فعل کی طرف منسوب ہو تو اسے وجوب کہتے ہیں ۔ تشریح قوله واعلم انهم جعلوا اقسام الحكم مرة الايجاب والخ مندرجہ بالا عبارت سے ماتن نے اصولیین کے کلام میں تاقع وتزاحم دکھایا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ حضرات اصولیین حکم کی قسموں کو کبھی ایجاب و تحریم کا نام دیتے ہیں جیسا کہ انقسام حکم کے مقام میں ابھی گذرا۔ اور کبھی حکم کی قسموں کو وجوب و حرمت سے تعبیر کرتے ہیں جیسا کہ فقہ کی تعریف "العلم بالا حکام

قوله فحمل بعضهم على المسامحة
اس عبارت سے کلام اصولیین میں واقع تزاحم کے ایک جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے۔ دفع تزاحم : یعنی کلام اصولیین میں جو بظاہر تزام وتدافع نظر آتا ہے اسکو دفع کرتے ہوئے بعض علماء نے کہا ہے کہ کلام اصولیین مسامحت پر محمول ہے۔
مسامحت کا معنی یہ ہے کہ مقسم (حکم) میں یا قسم ( وجوب وحرمت ) میں تاویل کی گئی ہے چنا نچہ کہا گیا ہے کہ ایجاب و تحریم کے بجائے وجوب وحرمت کا جہاں کہیں اطلاق ہوا ہے وہاں مقسم یعنی لفظ حکم غیر معنی حقیقی میں مستعمل ہے اور معنی حقیقی جو ایجاب وتحریم ہے مقصود نہیں ہوتا ، پس وجوب وحرمت کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے مقسم ما ثبت بالخطاب ہوگا۔ چنانچہ صدر الشریعہ نے توضیح میں کہا ہے۔ والفقهاء يطلقونه على ما يثبت بالكتاب كالوجوب والحرمة مجازاً بطريق اطلاق اسم المصدر على المصدر كالخلق على المخلوق لكن لما شاع فيه صار منقولا اصطلاحياً وهو حقيقة اصطلاحية_انتهى
یعنی فقہاء حکم کا اطلاق مجاز اس پر کرتے ہیں جو کتاب اللہ سے ثابت ہو جیسے وجوب وحرمت مصدر پر اسم مصدر کے اطلاق کے طریقے سے جیسے خلق کا اطلاق مخلوق پر لیکن جب اس کا استعمال اس میں شائع و ذائع ہو گیا تو وہ منقول اصطلاحی ہو گیا اور یہ حقیقت اصطلاحیہ ہے۔
الحاصل مقسم "ما ثبت بالخطاب ہے لہذا اسکی تقسیم وجوب و حرمت کی طرف درست ہے۔ یا یہ کہیں کہ وجوب و حرمت بولکر ایجاب و تحریم مرا دلیا ہے اس لئے کہ وجوب وحرمت ، ایجاب و تحریم کا اثر ہوتا ہے ، گویا مسبب ( یعنی وجوب و حرمت ) بولکر اسکا سبب ( یعنی ایجاب و تحریم ) مراد ہے اطلاق المسبب على السبب کے قبیل سے ہے یہ قسم میں تاویل ہے جبکہ پہلی تاویل مقسم کی تھی۔
قوله وبعضهم على انهما متحدان بالذات الخ
اس عبارت سے کلام اصولیین میں وقوع تدافع کا ، یہ دوسرا جواب مذکور ہے جو حضرت امام
Matn al-Muslim: 'And know that they made the divisions of hukm sometimes ijab and tahrim, and sometimes wujub and hurma. So some scholars carry it on leniency (musamaha), and some on the view that they are identical in essence and different in consideration. For the meaning of 'if'al' when attributed to the ruler is called ijab, and when attributed to the act is called wujub.'
Translation: And know that the usul scholars named the divisions of hukm also as ijab and tahrim, and also as wujub and hurma. So some scholars carried the speech of usul scholars on leniency (musamaha), and some on that wujub/hurma and ijab/tahrim are identical in essence and different in consideration because the meaning of 'if'al' when attributed to the ruler is called ijab, and when attributed to the act is called wujub.
Explanation: His saying 'And know that they made the divisions of hukm sometimes ijab and tahrim...' – From the above expression, the author (matn) shows contradiction and conflict in the speech of usul scholars, and says that the usul scholars sometimes name the divisions of hukm as ijab and tahrim, as passed in the place of division of hukm, and sometimes express the divisions of hukm as wujub and hurma, as in the definition of fiqh 'knowledge of the legal rulings' they mean by rulings wujub and hurma etc., and the conflict between ijab and wujub, tahrim and hurma is not hidden.
His saying: 'So some scholars carry it on leniency (musamaha)' – This expression points to one answer to the conflict occurring in the speech of usul scholars. Repelling the conflict: That is, some scholars, while repelling the apparent contradiction and inconsistency in the speech of usul scholars, said that the speech of usul scholars is carried on leniency (musamaha). The meaning of musamaha is that either the divided thing (maqsim) or the division (qism) is interpreted figuratively (ta'wil). Thus it is said that wherever wujub and hurma are used instead of ijab and tahrim, the word hukm (maqsim) is used in other than its real meaning, and the real meaning which is ijab and tahrim is not intended. So when relating to wujub and hurma, the maqsim will be 'what is established by address (khitab)'. As Sadr al-Shari'ah said in al-Tawdih: 'And the jurists use it (hukm) metaphorically for what is established by the Book, like wujub and hurma, by way of applying the name of the abstract noun (masdar) to the concrete noun (masdar), like applying 'khalq' to the created thing (makhluq). But when its usage became widespread, it became a transferred technical term (manqul istilahi), and it is a technical reality (haqiqah istilahiyyah).' End.
Conclusion: The maqsim is 'what is established by address', so its division into wujub and hurma is correct. Or one may say that by saying wujub and hurma, they intend ijab and tahrim, because wujub and hurma are the effect of ijab and tahrim. So by mentioning the effect (musabbab), the cause (sabab) is intended, which is of the type of applying the name of the effect to the cause (itlaq al-musabbab 'ala al-sabab). This is a ta'wil in the division (qism), whereas the first ta'wil was in the maqsim.
His saying: 'And some on the view that they are identical in essence (mutahidan bi al-dhat)...' – This expression mentions the second answer to the occurrence of contradiction in the speech of usul scholars, which is from Imam (Muhammad?)
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the twofold division of hukm in usul al-fiqh. The matn observes that scholars sometimes divide hukm into ijab (commanding) and tahrim (prohibiting), and sometimes into wujub (obligation) and hurma (prohibition). This creates an apparent contradiction. The commentator explains two reconciliations: (1) The first approach (musamaha) says that the term 'hukm' is used metaphorically (majaz) for wujub and hurma, or that wujub and hurma are used and ijab/tahrim are intended either by interpreting the maqsim (hukm) or by interpreting the division (using effect for cause). (2) The second approach says that ijab/tahrim and wujub/hurma are identical in essence (mutahidan bi al-dhat) but differ in consideration: ijab is the attribution of obligation to the Lawgiver, wujub is its attribution to the act. This is a common Hanafi position distinguishing the source and the effect. The commentator also quotes Sadr al-Shari'ah to show that 'hukm' is used technically to mean the established rule (like wujub and hurma) as a transferred technical term.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ijab): The act of the Lawgiver in commanding (making something obligatory), attributed to Him
وجوب (wujub): The quality of obligation in the act itself, as a result of ijab
تحریم (tahrim): The act of the Lawgiver in prohibiting, attributed to Him
حرمة (hurma): The quality of prohibition in the act
مسامحت (musamaha): Leniency in interpretation; carrying the term figuratively to resolve contradiction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, one way to reconcile the twofold division is that using 'wujub/hurma' instead of 'ijab/tahrim' is an example of:
A) Applying the name of the cause to the effect
B) Applying the name of the effect to the cause
C) Literal usage (haqiqah)
D) Abrogation (naskh)

Key Takeaway
Ijab and wujub, and tahrim and hurma, are identical in essence; the distinction is one of attribution, not of ruling.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
8 min
Resolving the Contradiction in Hukm's Classification
Two solutions to the conflict between usul and fiqh definitions of hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the apparent contradiction between the usul and fiqh definitions of hukm and EXPLAIN two compatible solutions: musamaha (tolerance via ta'wil) and ittihad bi al-dhat (identity in essence) with their justifications.

Core Idea
The apparent contradiction between usul and fiqh definitions of hukm is resolved by either metaphorical tolerance (musamaha) or identity-in-essence (ittihad bi al-dhat).

In usul al-fiqh, hukm is defined as the address of Allah (khitab) that commands or prohibits, leading to classifications like ijab and tahrim. Yet in fiqh, hukm is often applied to the resulting qualities of acts, such as wujub (obligation) and hurmah (prohibition). This appears contradictory when the same term 'hukm' is divided differently. This lesson explores two elegant solutions from the Hanafi tradition that reconcile this terminological tension, allowing us to see deeper consistency in the conceptual framework of the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Read the opening lines of the passage. State the difference: usul defines hukm as the divine address (ijab/tahrim), while fiqh uses hukm for the resulting qualities of the act (wujub/hurmah). How can the same term be divided into two different sets?
The contradiction is only apparent; it arises from different perspectives.
2
Understand the first solution: musamaha
Follow the author's explanation of musamaha. Notice two sub-solutions: (a) hukm is used metaphorically in fiqh to mean 'what is established by the address', making the division into wujub/hurmah direct; (b) the terms wujub/hurmah are used metonymically for their causes ijab/tahrim (effect-for-cause). Both are ta'wil.
Sadr al-Shari'ah's quote from al-Tawdih supports this metaphorical usage as a transmitted technical reality.
3
Understand the second solution: ittihad bi al-dhat
Study Imam Razi's approach: ijab and wujub are identical in essence, differing only in consideration (i'tibar). When the meaning of the command 'Do!' is attributed to the commander, it is ijab; when attributed to the act, it is wujub. Thus the usul and fiqh definitions consider different aspects of the same reality.
This is a metaphysical resolution emphasizing the unity of the divine command.
4
Compare and contrast the two solutions
List the key features of each. Musamaha relies on linguistic tolerance (majaz/majaz), while ittihad relies on ontological identity with multiple aspects. Both are accepted in Hanafi sharh, though they have different implications for understanding the nature of hukm.
The text notes that the author of the matn adds a long gloss affirming that hukm is the address itself and wujub is not a real quality of the act.
5
Reflect on the Hanafi position
Consider how these solutions preserve the consistency of the usul-fiqh relationship. Understand that the apparent contradiction is not a flaw but a richness of the tradition. The Hanafi school accommodates both approaches, showing flexibility in conceptual analysis.
This lesson builds skill in identifying terminological shifts and resolving them through principled hermeneutics.

انمايدل على أن الفعل لا يتصف بصفة حقيقية قائمة به واما انه لايتصف بصفة اعتبارية وهو كونه بحيث يتعلق به الايجاب فالدليل لايدل عليه بل هذا هو الظاهر فيكون كل من الموجب والواجب متصفاً بماهو قائم به حقيقة فتأمل اقول ان الوجوب ليس صفة للفعل الخارجي في مرتبة حقيقته بل انما يحدث بالجعل مقدماً عليه والمعدوم مادام
In the subsidiary [branches of fiqh], by 'ahkam' they mean wujub and hurmah, etc., and the contradiction between ijab and wujub and between tahrim and hurmah is not hidden. His saying: 'So some of them carried it upon musamaha.' This phrase indicates one of the answers to the contradiction occurring in the speech of the usul scholars. Repelling the contradiction: Meaning, in the speech of the usul scholars, what appears outwardly as contradiction and conflict, some scholars, while repelling it, said that the speech of the usul scholars is carried upon musamaha (tolerance/approximation). The meaning of musamaha is that a ta'wil (interpretive explanation) is made either in the divided term (hukm) or in the division (wujub and hurmah). Thus it is said that wherever wujub and hurmah are used instead of ijab and tahrim, the divided term—i.e., the word hukm—is used in a non-real meaning, and the real meaning which is ijab and tahrim is not intended. So, when relating to wujub and hurmah, the divided term becomes 'what is established by the address' (ma thabata bi al-khitab). Accordingly, Sadr al-Shari'ah said in al-Tawdih: 'And the jurists use it (hukm) metaphorically for what is established by the Book, like wujub and hurmah, by way of applying the name of the masdar to the masdar, like khalq (creation) upon makhluq (created). But when its usage became widespread, it became a transmitted technical term, and it is a technical reality.' End quote. So the summary: The divided term is 'what is established by the address'; therefore its division into wujub and hurmah is correct. Or one may say that by saying wujub and hurmah, they intend ijab and tahrim, because wujub and hurmah are the effects of ijab and tahrim; so the effect (wujub and hurmah) is mentioned but the cause (ijab and tahrim) is intended—this is from the category of applying the caused to the cause. This is a ta'wil in the division, while the first ta'wil was in the divided term. His saying: 'And some [held] that they are identical in essence, etc.' This phrase mentions the second answer to the occurrence of contradiction in the speech of the usul scholars, which Imam Razi gave in al-Mahsul: that the contradictory speech of the usul scholars is carried upon identity in essence and difference in consideration. Therefore, making ijab and wujub and tahrim and hurmah divisions of hukm is not wrong. Its detail is that ijab and wujub, and similarly tahrim and hurmah—each of the two types is identical in essence and different in consideration. That is, wujub is identical with ijab in essence, and hurmah with tahrim. If there is any difference between ijab and wujub or between tahrim and hurmah, it is only by consideration. Since hukm is the meaning and purport of the command 'Do!' of Allah, may His glory be exalted, and it is clear that at that time, there is no real quality for the act related to the command that could be called wujub or hurmah, because the act is non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be qualified by a real quality. So at that time only the quality of the commander—the meaning of 'Do!'—exists, which subsists in the Essence of Allah. So, when this quality of the meaning of 'Do!' is attributed to the commander, this meaning is expressed as ijab; and when this same quality of the meaning of 'Do!' is attributed to the act that is the object of 'Do!', it is called wujub. The summary is that ijab and wujub are both the meaning of 'Do!', but from the aspect that this meaning resides with the commander, it is ijab, and from the aspect that this meaning relates to the act, it is wujub. So, in the place of division, what the usul scholars named as ijab and tahrim is according to the first consideration, and in the fiqh definition, what is meant by ahkam—taken as wujub and hurmah—is according to the second consideration. Hence, there is no contradiction in their speech. At this point, the author has written a long gloss under his statement fa-inna ma'na if'al idh unsiba. And it is verified that hukm is the very address of Allah; ijab is the very saying 'Do!', and there is no real quality for the act from it, because the saying does not have a quality for its object due to its relation to the non-existent. And when it is attributed to the commander... and it is objected that what was mentioned only indicates that the act is not qualified by a real quality subsisting in it; but as for it not being qualified by an attributive quality—that it is such that ijab attaches to it—the evidence does not indicate that. Rather, this is the apparent meaning; thus each of the 'obligator' (al-mujib) and 'obligated' (al-wajib) is qualified by what subsists in it in reality. So reflect. I say that wujub is not a quality of the external act in the stage of its reality; rather, it occurs by the making (al-ja'l) as something previous to it, and the non-existent as long as...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut is a sharh on Musallam al-Thubut, addressing an apparent contradiction in usul al-fiqh terminology. The issue: In usul, 'hukm' is often defined as the address of Allah (khitab Allah) which entails ijab (commanding) and tahrim (prohibiting). Yet in fiqh, 'hukm' is applied to the resulting qualities like wujub (obligation) and hurmah (prohibition). This creates a seeming conflict in classification when hukm is divided into wujub and hurmah. The text presents two solutions:

1. Musamaha (tolerance): This approach accepts a ta'wil either in the divided term (hukm) or in the division (wujub/hurmah). In the first ta'wil, the word 'hukm' is used metaphorically (majaz) in the fiqh definition to mean 'what is established by the address', not the address itself. This is supported by Sadr al-Shari'ah's quote from al-Tawdih, where he explains that jurists use 'hukm' metaphorically for rulings like wujub, similar to calling the created thing 'creation'. Over time, this became a technical reality. In the second ta'wil, the terms wujub and hurmah are used metonymically to refer to their causes, ijab and tahrim (applying the effect to the cause).

2. Identity in essence (ittihad bi al-dhat) with difference in consideration (ikhtilaf bi al-i'tibar): This is the solution of Imam Razi (from the Shafi'i school) in al-Mahsul. According to this, ijab and wujub are not separate entities; they are the same reality considered from different angles. When we look at the divine command 'Do!' as a quality subsisting in Allah, it is called ijab. When we look at that same command as it relates to the act (which becomes obligated), it is called wujub. Similarly for tahrim and hurmah. Thus, the usul definition (division into ijab/tahrim) looks at the command relative to the Lawgiver, while the fiqh definition (division into wujub/hurmah) looks at it relative to the act. Since the two definitions consider different aspects, there is no real contradiction.

The text then includes a further verification and objection, noting that wujub is not a real quality of the act but an attributive relation that arises from the divine making. This aligns with the Hanafi position that the non-existent can bear legal attributes through the legislator's address.

Overall, the passage teaches us to carefully distinguish between the source (Allah's address) and the effect (the resulting obligation/prohibition) when discussing hukm. The Hanafi usul tradition, as represented here, accommodates both the metaphysical reality of the divine command and the practical application in fiqh.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The quality of the divine command 'Do!' as it subsists in the commander.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The quality of the act being obligatory, considered as the effect of ijab and attributed to the act.
المسامحة (al-musamaha): A tolerant interpretation that allows using 'hukm' metaphorically for the effect, or using the effect to mean the cause.
الاتحاد بالذات (al-ittihad bi al-dhat): Identity in essence: ijab and wujub are the same reality considered differently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the main difference between the usul and fiqh definitions of 'hukm'?
A) Usul defines hukm as the address of Allah, while fiqh defines it as the quality of the act.
B) Usul defines hukm as ijab and tahrim, while fiqh defines it as wujub and hurmah.
C) Usul uses hukm for commands only, fiqh uses it for prohibitions only.
D) There is no difference; it is the same definition in both fields.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction between the usul and fiqh definitions of hukm is resolved by recognizing either metaphorical usage (musamaha) or identity-in-essence with difference in consideration (ittihad bi al-dhat).

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
Ijab and Wujub: One Reality, Two Considerations
Resolution of an usul dispute on hukm categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between ijab and wujub and apply it to resolve apparent contradictions in usul al-fiqh classifications.

Core Idea
Ijab and wujub are one reality; difference is only by consideration — Ruler vs. act.

In usul al-fiqh, scholars sometimes classify ijab (the act of making obligatory) and wujub (obligation itself) as separate categories of hukm. Yet other times they treat them as interchangeable. This seems contradictory. This passage, from a Hanafi commentary on Muslim al-Thubut, resolves the puzzle by showing that both terms refer to the same divine command 'do' (if'al) under different relations. Understanding this resolution is essential for correctly mapping the categories of hukm and avoiding confusion in higher-level usul discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Notice that usul scholars list 'ijaab' as a category of hukm, while fuqaha use 'wujoob' for the same ruling. These seem like different entities. Locate this tension in the passage.
The passage opens by citing Razi in al-Mahsul that the discourse of usuliyyun is reconcilable.
2
Understand the principle of unity in essence
The passage asserts that ijab and wujub are united in essence (muttaḥid bi al-dhāt). They are not two different things but the same meaning (ma'na) of the command 'do'.
Same applies to tahrim and hurmah.
3
Trace the two considerations
When this meaning is attributed to the Ruler (Allah), it is called ijab. When attributed to the act of the mukallaf, it is called wujub. This is a difference in consideration (i'tibar), not essence.
The passage says: 'إذ نسب إلى الحاكم فهو إيجاب، وإذ نسب إلى الفعل فهو وجوب'
4
Examine the objection by Mirza Jan
Mirza Jan objects that even if wujub is not a real attribute, it remains an i'tibari attribute of the act, so unity is not proven. Understand his logical point.
The objection is that the act still has a relation (talluq) to ijab, making it distinct.
5
Follow the author's response
The author responds that wujub is not an attribute of the external act at all; it is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of ijab that precedes the act. Since the act is non-existent before the command, it cannot have even an i'tibari attribute that inheres. Wujub is entirely abstract (i'tibari) from ijab, so the two are identical in existence.
Key phrase: 'فوجود افعل هو الإيجاب وهو الوجوب إلا بالاعتبار'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رازی نے محصول میں دیا ہے کہ اصولیین کا کلام مزاحم ، اتحاد ذاتی اور اختلاف اعتباری پر محمول ہے اس لئے ایجاب ووجوب اور تحریم و حرمت کو اقسام حکم قرار دینا غلط نہیں۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ ایجاب و وجوب اور اسی طرح تحریم و حرمت دونوں نوع میں سے ہر ایک متحد بالذات ہے اور مختلف بالاعتبار ہے، یعنی وجوب، ایجاب کے ساتھ اور حرمت تحریم کے ساتھ بالذات متحد ہیں اگر ایجاب ووجوب کے درمیان یا تحریم و حرمت کے مابین کچھ فرق ہے بھی تو وہ اعتباری ہے۔ چونکہ حکم“ وہ اللہ عز شانہ تعالیٰ کے ارشاد فعل“ کا معنی و مفاد ہے اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ اسوقت فعل سے متعلق فعل کے لئے کوئی صفت حقیقیہ نہیں تھی کہ اسے وجوب و حرمت کا نام دیا جا سکے کیونکہ نفل ہی معدوم ہے اور معدوم کسی صفت حقیقیہ سے متصف نہیں ہو سکتا ، پس اسوقت صرف حاکم کی صفت معنی افعل موجود ہے جو ذات باری تعالیٰ کے ساتھ قائم ہے چنانچہ اس صفت معنی افعل کی نسبت جب حاکم کی طرف کیجائے تو اس معنی کو ایجاب سے تعبیر کرتے ہیں اور جب یہی صفت معنی افعل ، افعل کے متعلق فعل کی طرف منسوب ہو تو اسے وجوب کہتے ہیں حاصل یہ کہ ایجاب و وجوب دونوں افعل ہی کا معنی ہے لیکن اس اعتبار سے کہ اس معنی کا قیام حاکم کے ساتھ ہے تو ایجاب ہے اور اس اعتبار سے کہ اس معنی کا تعلق فعل سے ہے تو وجوب ہے پس مقام تقسیم میں اصولیین نے جو ایجاب و تحریم کا نام دیا ہے وہ با اعتبار اول ہے اور تعریف فقہ میں احکام سے مراد جو وجوب و حرمت لیا ہے وہ باعتبار ثانی ہے لہذا انکے کلام میں تدافع نہیں ہے اس مقام پر ماتن نے اپنے قول فان معنی افعل اذ انسب کے ضمن میں طویل حاشیہ آرائی کی ہے۔
وحقق بان الحكم نفس خطاب الله تعالى فالايجاب هو نفس قوله افعل وليس للفعل منه صفة حقيقية فان القول ليس لمتعلقه منه صفة لتعلقه بالمعدوم وهو اذا نسب الى الحاكم الخ و اورد عليه بأن ماذكر انمايدل على أن الفعل لا يتصف بصفة حقيقية قائمة به واما انه لايتصف بصفة اعتبارية وهو كونه بحيث يتعلق به الايجاب فالدليل لايدل عليه بل هذا هو الظاهر فيكون كل من الموجب والواجب متصفاً بماهو قائم به حقيقة فتأمل اقول ان الوجوب ليس صفة للفعل الخارجي في مرتبة حقيقته بل انما يحدث بالجعل مقدماً عليه والمعدوم مادام
انتهى
معدوماً لايتصف بصفة ثبوتية حادثة وح لاحظ للفعل من الوجود الاوجود افعل متعلقابه تعلق الطلب بالمعدوم لان الانتزاعى لا وجود له الابوجود المنتزع عنه فوجود افعل هو الايجاب وهو الوجوب الابالاعتبار تدبر خلاصہ:۔ حاشیہ کی عبارت کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ محق شارح مختصر الاصول کی تحقیق یہ ہے کہ ایجاب وجوب دونوں متحد بالذات ہیں اور بالاعتبار متغائر ہیں اس لئے کہ خطاب وایجاب حاکم کی صفت ہے اور فعل مکلف کے متعلق ہے پس اس کی اضافت حاکم کی طرف ہونے کے اعتبار سے اسکو ایجاب کہتے ہیں اور اس کی اضافت فعل کی طرف ہونے کے اعتبار سے اس کو وجوب کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت دونوں کی ایک ہے اور فرق اعتباری ہے۔ فاضل کا اعتراض : مگر محقق شارح مختصر الاصول کے اس نظر یہ پر فاضل مرزا جان نے اعتراض کیا ہے جس کو مصنف نے اور دعلیہ بان ماذکر سے بیان کیا ہے۔ اعتراض کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ تحقیق مذکور سے زیادہ سے زیادہ یہ ظاہر ہوتا ہے کہ فعل صفت حقیقیہ یعنی وجوب کے ساتھ متصف نہیں ہوسکتا ہے بلکہ فعل کے لئے ایسا وجوب حاصل ہوتا ہے جو صفت اعتبار یہ ہے اور اس سے مطلوب کا ثبوت نہیں ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ مطلوب و مدعی صفت حقیقیہ کی نفی سے اتحاد کا ثبوت ہے اور وجوب جو فعل کے لئے امرا اعتباری ہے سے اتحاد لازم نہیں آتا ہے۔
جواب :۔ مصنف نے اپنے قول اقول أن الوجوب ليس صفة للفعل الخارجي“ سے فاضل مرزا جان کے اعتراض کا جواب دیا ہے۔
جواب کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ وجوب فعل خارجی کی صفت حقیقیہ نہیں ہے جو کہ اس کے ساتھ قائم ہو اس لئے کہ وجوب ایجاب کا ایسا لازم ہے جو اس سے منفک نہیں ہوتا اور اسمیں کوئی شک نہیں ہے کہ ایجاب مکلف کے فعل سے پہلے ہے تو ضروری ہے کہ وجوب بھی فعل مکلف سے پہلے ہو غرض فعل معدوم کو صفت وجوب میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ معدوم صفت حقیقیہ سے متصف نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ وجوب امرانتزاعی ہے اور امر انتزاعی کا وجود متزع عنہ و منشاء کے وجود ہے ہے پس وجود افعل وہ ایجاب ہے اور وہی وجوب ہے مگر بالا عتبار اسکی مزید توضیح یہ ہے کہ وجوب فعل واقعی خارجی کی صفت
Razi stated in al-Mahsul that the discourse of the usuliyyun is reconcilable, being based on unity in essence and difference in consideration. Therefore, making ijab and wujub, and tahrim and hurmah categories of hukm is not wrong. The detail is: ijab and wujub, and similarly tahrim and hurmah, each of the two types is united in essence and different in consideration. That is, wujub and ijab are essentially united, likewise hurmah and tahrim. If there is any difference between ijab and wujub or between tahrim and hurmah, it is by consideration. Since 'hukm' is the meaning and import of the address of Allah of the act 'do', and it is clear that at that moment (before the act), the act does not have any real attribute such that it could be called wujub or hurmah, because the act is non-existent, and a non-existent cannot be described with a real attribute. Therefore, at that time, only the attribute of the meaning 'do' exists, which subsists with the Essence of Allah. So when this meaning 'do' is attributed to the Ruler, it is called ijab; and when this same meaning 'do' is attributed to the act, it is called wujub. Summary: ijab and wujub are both the meaning of 'do', but by the consideration that this meaning subsists with the Ruler, it is ijab; and by the consideration that this meaning relates to the act, it is wujub. So in the place of division (of hukm), the usuliyyun named ijab and tahrim by the first consideration, and in fiqh, by hukm they mean wujub and hurmah by the second consideration. Thus there is no contradiction in their words. At this point, the author (matn) has written a lengthy hashia under his statement 'fa inna ma'na if'al idh unsiba'. And he verified that 'hukm is the same as the address of Allah, so ijab is the same as His saying 'do', and the act does not have a real attribute from it (the address) because the address does not have an attribute from its object due to its relation to the non-existent. When it is attributed to the Ruler...' and it was objected that what was mentioned only indicates that the act is not described with a real attribute subsisting in it; but that it is not described with a consideration attribute, namely its being such that ijab relates to it, the proof does not indicate that; rather, this (that it is described with a consideration attribute) is apparent. Thus each of the obligator (mujib) and the obligatory (wajib) is described with what subsists in it actually (haqiqatan). So reflect. I say: wujub is not an attribute of the external act at the level of its reality; rather, it occurs by positive command (ja'l) prior to it, and the non-existent, as long as it is non-existent, is not described with a new positive attribute. And the act has no share of existence except the existence of 'do' related to it, like the relation of a request to the non-existent, because the abstract (i'tibari) has no existence except the existence of that from which it is abstracted. So the existence of 'do' is ijab, and it is wujub, except by consideration. Summary: The summarizer's (hashiya) conclusion is that the research of the muhaqqiq (commentator of Mukhtasar al-Usul) is that ijab and wujub are united in essence and different by consideration, because the address and ijab are an attribute of the Ruler and relate to the act of the mukallaf. So when their attribution is to the Ruler, it is called ijab; and when their attribution is to the act, it is called wujub. The reality of both is one, and the difference is by consideration. Objection by Fadil Mirza Jan: The muhaqqiq's view is objected to by Fadil Mirza Jan, which the author mentioned with 'wa 'uriḍa 'alayhi bimā dhakara'. The objection's gist: The mentioned research at most shows that the act cannot be described with a real attribute (wujub); rather, a wujub accrues to the act that is a consideration attribute, and this does not prove what is required, because what is sought (and claimed) is proof of unity through negation of a real attribute; and wujub, which is a consideration attribute for the act, does not necessitate unity. Response: The author responded to Mirza Jan's objection with his statement 'aqūlu inna al-wujūba laysa ṣifatan l'il-fi'li al-khāriji'. The gist of the response: Wujub is not a real attribute of the external act that subsists in it, because wujub is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of ijab that is inseparable from it, and there is no doubt that ijab precedes the act of the mukallaf. So it is necessary that wujub also precedes the act. In short, the non-existent act has no role in the attribute of wujub, because the non-existent is not described with a real attribute, since wujub is a relational/abstract matter (amr i'tibari), and the existence of an abstract matter is only by the existence of that from which it is abstracted (munsha'). Thus the existence of 'do' is ijab, and it is the same as wujub, but by consideration. Further clarification: Wujub is not an attribute of the actual external act.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Nu'ut (a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut) addresses a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute: how can ijab (the act of making obligatory) and wujub (obligation itself) be considered separate categories of hukm when they seem to refer to the same reality? The author reconciles this by distinguishing between two relations of a single meaning (ma'na) inherent in Allah's command 'do' (if'al). When that meaning is considered as subsisting in the Ruler (Allah), it is called ijab; when considered as relating to the act, it is called wujub. This solves the apparent contradiction: usul scholars use ijab/tahrim in their division of hukm (first consideration), while fuqaha refer to wujub/hurmah (second consideration). The passage then discusses Mirza Jan's objection: even according to this view, the act still has an attribute (wujub), albeit a consideration attribute, so the claimed unity (ijab = wujub) is not established. The author responds by asserting that wujub is not a real attribute of the external act but a necessary concomitant of ijab that precedes the act. Since the act is non-existent before the command, it cannot have real attributes; wujub is purely relational (i'tibari), having no existence independent of the command from which it is abstracted. Thus, the existence of the command (ijab) is itself the existence of obligation (wujub); they differ only in how we consider them. This is a sophisticated Hanafi solution preserving the unity of the divine attribute while accounting for linguistic and legal terminology.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijaab): The attribute of the Ruler (Allah) consisting of the meaning of the command 'do' considered in relation to the Commander.
الوجوب (al-wujoob): The same meaning of 'do' considered in relation to the act, i.e., the quality of being obligatory.
صفت حقیقیہ (ṣifat ḥaqīqiyya): A real attribute that subsists in an entity; the act before existence lacks such an attribute.
اعتباری (iʿtibārī): Relational or abstract, having no independent existence but existing by virtue of being abstracted from its source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between ijab and wujub?
A) They are entirely different and unrelated categories.
B) They are essentially the same but differ in consideration (relation to Ruler vs. act).
C) Ijab is the cause and wujub is the effect.
D) Wujub is a real attribute of the act, while ijab is not.

Key Takeaway
Ijab and wujub are one reality — the divine command 'do' — distinguished only by whether we relate it to the Commander or to the commanded act.

The Unity of Ijab and Wujub Under Different Considerations
Defending the identity of ijab and wujub against philosophical objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi claim that ijab and wujub are essentially identical, and defend it against objections using the concept of considerations (i'tibar).

Core Idea
Ijab and wujub are one reality considered from two aspects—the ruler's command and the act's quality.

In this passage, the commentator defends the earlier claim that ijab (the act of imposing obligation) and wujub (the quality of being obligatory) are not two separate entities but the same reality. The objection is that wujub follows ijab, implying a cause-effect relationship between distinct things. The response uses a foundational Hanafi usul principle: a single entity can have multiple considerations (i'tibarat) without becoming multiple realities. The text also addresses a philosophical objection from Aristotelian categories, showing that mental categories can overlap under different considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that wujub follows ijab, so they cannot be one—otherwise a thing would precede itself. Also, ijab belongs to the category of action and wujub to passion, which are mutually exclusive.
The objection relies on a strict reading of causality and Aristotelian categories.
2
Understand the response via considerations
The response allows a thing to be dependent on itself under another consideration. That is, the dependence is between the two considerations (the act of imposing and the quality of being obligatory), not between two separate realities.
This preserves unity of source and referent while acknowledging a conceptual distinction.
3
Assess the categorical objection
The claim that categories are mutually exclusive is debated. Even if real categories cannot coincide, mental categories under different considerations can. The objection from Ibn Sina's Al-Shifa is dismissed because it does not apply to mental considerations.
Hanafi usul treats categories as conceptual tools, not ontologically rigid.
4
Recognize the broader principle
This argument exemplifies the Hanafi approach of using nuanced distinctions (i'tibarat) to resolve apparent contradictions in defining the hukm shar'i.
The same method applies to other pairs like tahrim and hurmah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أورد أن الوجوب يترتب على الإيجاب فكيف الاتحاد ويجاب بجواز ترتب الشيء على نفسه باعتبار آخر ومرجعه إلى ترتب أحد الاعتبارين على الآخر. قال السيد: وبهذا يجاب عما قيل إن الإيجاب من مقولة الفعل والوجوب من مقولة الانفعال ودعوى امتناع صدق المقولات على شيء باعتبار شيء محل مناقشة. أقول: الحاصل أن تصادق المقولات الحقيقة لم يلزم وتصادق الاعتبارية باعتبارات مختلفة ليس بممتنع فلا يرد ما قيل أن الشيخ في الشفاء صرح بأن المقولات متباينة فلا يتصادفان ولو باعتبار.
It was objected that wujub follows ijab, so how can there be unity? And it is answered by the permissibility of a thing being dependent on itself under another consideration, and its reference is to the dependence of one of the two considerations on the other. Al-Sayyid said: By this is answered what was said that ijab is from the category of action and wujub is from the category of passion, and the claim that it is impossible for categories to be true of a thing under some consideration is debatable. I say: The conclusion is that there is no necessary simultaneous truth of real categories, and the simultaneous truth of mental categories under different considerations is not impossible. So the objection that the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) stated in al-Shifa that categories are mutually exclusive so they cannot coincide even under consideration does not stand.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the earlier claim that ijab (the act of imposing an obligation) and wujub (the quality of being obligatory) are essentially identical. The objection argues that since wujub (the effect) follows ijab (the cause), they cannot be the same thing—otherwise a thing would precede itself. The response clarifies that a single reality can be considered from different aspects: as a speech act attributed to the ruler (ijab) and as a quality attributed to the act (wujub). The precedence is not of the reality itself but of one consideration over another. A further objection from Aristotelian categories (action vs. passion) is dismissed because these categories are not real in the same sense when applied to mental considerations; simultaneous truth of different considerations is unproblematic. This reasoning is typical of Hanafi usul, which often employs nuanced distinctions to defend the coherence of its concepts.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The act of imposing an obligation, attributed to the ruler (hakim) as a speech act.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The quality of being obligatory, attributed to the act (fi'l) as a property.
الاتحاد (al-ittihad): Unity in source (mansha') and referent (musadaq), not in concept or consideration.
المقولات (al-muqulat): The Aristotelian categories (action, passion, etc.) which are here argued to be mental constructs that can overlap under different considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because one thing can be dependent on itself under different considerations.
C) Because ijab and wujub are both mental fictions.
D) Because the categories of action and passion are identical in reality.

Key Takeaway
Ijab and wujub are identical in source and referent, differing only in consideration; this resolves apparent contradictions and is foundational to Hanafi usul.

The Unity of Ijab and Wujub: Defending Hanafi Usul Against Category Objections
Defending unity of ijab/wujub via category distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that ijab and wujub are one in essence and defend it by distinguishing between real and accidental categories (maqulat haqiqiya vs i'tibariya) and applying the concept of i'tibar (consideration).

Core Idea
Ijab and wujub are one essence under accidental categories; they differ only by consideration.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the obligation (ijab) imposed by the Lawgiver and the resulting binding force (wujub) are not two separate realities. This passage from a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut defends that unity against objections rooted in the Aristotelian ten categories. The author, building on al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani's response, uses the concept of i'tibar (consideration) and a crucial distinction between real categories (maqulat haqiqiya) and accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya) to show that a single essence can fall under different categories by different considerations without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the claim: ijab and wujub are one in essence (muttaḥid bi al-dhāt) and differ only by i'tibar (consideration). Understand that this is a philosophical defense of the unity of the hukm.
This position is foundational to Hanafi usul: the act of obligating and the state of being obligated are the same reality.
2
Analyze the objection from distinct categories
Recognize the objection: ijab belongs to the category of action (fi'l) and wujub to passion (infi'āl). Since categories are mutually exclusive, they cannot be the same essence. Understand the threat: a single thing cannot have two genera in one station.
The objection draws on Ibn Sina's division of categories as essentially distinct.
3
Examine the primary Hanafi response
The response: a single thing can fall under two distinct categories by different considerations, but only for accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya), not real categories (maqulat haqīqiya). Real categories would indeed be problematic, but the Hanafi claim concerns accidental categories.
This distinction is the key: accidental categories are not real genera; they are mental considerations.
4
Address the three further objections
Work through each objection: (1) Ibn Sina's statement that real categories are essentially distinct; (2) the impossibility of two genera in one station; (3) the claim that essence and essential attributes cannot differ by consideration. For each, see how the response invokes the accidental/real distinction and notes that kalam nafsi (inner speech) belongs to quality, not action/passion.
These objections are found in the text after 'qāla aqūlu'.
5
Synthesize the resolution
Conclude: The unity of ijab and wujub is saved because the categories in question (action/passion) are applied accidentally (i'tibariyan) to a single essence, and in the specific case of kalam nafsi, it is from quality, avoiding the conflict altogether. Thus the Hanafi view is rationally sound.
This resolution preserves the integrity of the hukm as a single attribute.

اگر چہ اعتبارات مختلف ہوں یعنی اعتبارات سے ذات وذاتی کا اختلاف نہیں ہوتا بلکہ خواہ کوئی اعتبار کریں ذات وذاتی محفوظ رہتی ہے۔ جواب :۔ مندرجہ بالا اعتراضات کے جواب میں سید قدس سرہ کی جانب سے جواب دیتے ہوئے مصنف کہتے ہیں کہ سید قدس سرہ نے جو یہ کہا کہ دو مقولہ متبا ئنہ کا صدق جائز ہے تو اس قول سے ان کی مراد مقولہ حقیقیہ نہیں ہے کہ شئی واحد کے لئے مرتبہ واحدہ میں تعدد جنس ہونا لا زم آئے ایسا قول وہ کیونکر کر سکتے ہیں جسکا بطلان بدیہی ہے صحیح یہ ہے کہ اس سے ان کی مراد مقولہ اعتبار یہ ہے معنی یہ ہے کہ اعتبارات مختلفہ کے رو سے مقولات اعتباریہ کا صدق ایک شئی پر ممتنع نہیں ہے بلکہ جائز ہے پس زیر بحث مسئلہ میں ایجاب و وجوب کے اتحاد کا الزام جو ہم پر لازم آ رہا ہے وہ لازم نہیں ہے کیونکہ خطاب نفسی جو در حقیقت کلام نفسی ہے اصولیین کے نزدیک مقولہ کیف سے ہے ” مقولہ فعل یا مقولہ انفعال سے نہیں ہے اور اگر کلام نفسی یا خطاب نفسی کو بھی مقولہ فعل س یا مقولہ انفعال سے کہ دیتے ہیں تو بالا عتبار یا مجازاً
It happens that the essence is united in reality in both considerations, but it differs by consideration, and due to this difference by consideration, the secondary is ordered upon the primary. For example, man: he is ordered in respect of heat, and the same man is ordered in respect of movement, i.e., man in respect of heat is exactly the same man in respect of movement ordered. In short, a thing can be ordered upon itself, and that is because its reference is to the ordering of one of the two considerations upon the other, not that the ordering of a thing upon itself is real and essential, which would necessitate the ordering of a thing upon itself, as the objector imagined. His statement: 'al-Sayyid said: and by this, what is said that ijab is from the category, etc.' That is, al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, after mentioning the aforementioned objection and response in his Hashiyah Sharh Mukhtasar, said that this same response is also given in answer to the objection that is made in this place from another aspect. Objection: To invalidate the unity between ijab and wujub, this objection is made from another aspect: that ijab and wujub fall under two distinct categories. Thus, ijab is from the category of action, and wujub is from the category of passion, and categories are essentially distinct from each other. So how can the statement of unity be correct between two things that fall under two distinct categories? Otherwise, a single thing would require having two genera in a single station, whereas having two genera for a single thing in a single station is impossible. Response: The response to this objection is what has preceded: that a single thing can fall under two distinct categories by two different considerations, because by different considerations, the application of accidental categories ([maqulat i'tibariya]) to a single thing is not impossible. That is, there is no impossibility that a single thing, by one consideration, falls under one category, and then that same thing, by another consideration, falls under another category. And saying that the application of categories to a single thing by different considerations is impossible is a point of discussion, because by different considerations, the application of categories to a single thing is permissible, not impossible. His statement: 'I say: the summary is that the co-application of real categories is not necessitated, etc.' By this expression, the author (matn) responded to several objections that arose on the statement of Sayyid al-Jurjani: 'and his saying that the impossibility of applying categories to a thing by different considerations is a point of discussion.' (1) The first objection is that which the author indicated by his following statement: 'then there is no escape from what preceded that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) in al-Shifa' explicitly stated that categories are essentially distinct, so they cannot apply to a single thing even by different considerations; rather, their instances would also be different. Hence, there is no discussion in the claim that the application of categories is impossible. So how did al-Sayyid say that the claim of impossibility is a point of discussion? (2) The second objection is that for a single thing to have two genera in a single station is prohibited, yet on the assumption of the unity of ijab and wujub, it necessitates that a single thing has two genera in a single station, because ijab is from the category of action and wujub from the category of passion, and categories are distinct genera. (3) The third objection is that the difference of essence and essential attribute is impossible even if considerations are different, i.e., considerations do not cause difference in essence and essential attribute; rather, regardless of the consideration, essence and essential attribute are preserved. Response: In response to the above objections, answering on behalf of Sayyid (may his secret be sanctified), the author says: When al-Sayyid said that the application of two distinct categories is permissible, he did not mean real categories, which would necessitate multiple genera for a single thing in a single station—how could he say such a thing whose invalidity is self-evident? Rather, he meant accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya). That is, the application of accidental categories to a single thing by different considerations is not impossible but permissible. Thus, in the issue under discussion, the charge of the unity of ijab and wujub that is leveled against us is not necessitated, because khatab nafsi (address of the soul), which is in reality kalam nafsi (speech of the soul), is according to the usuliyun from the category of quality, not from the category of action or passion. And if kalam nafsi or khatab nafsi is also said to be from the category of action or passion, then it is by consideration or metaphorically.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the issue of the unity of ijab (obligation) and wujub (necessity/obligation) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author is responding to objections that argue against this unity based on the Aristotelian categories (maqulat). The passage begins by explaining that a single essence can be considered differently, and thus one thing can be ordered upon itself by different considerations, without implying a real self-predication. Then it presents an objection from al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani's Hashiyah: ijab (action) and wujub (passion) belong to distinct categories, so they cannot be one essence. The response clarifies that a single thing can fall under different categories by different considerations, and this is permissible for accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya), not real categories (maqulat haqiqiya). Three further objections are raised: (1) Ibn Sina stated that real categories are essentially distinct and cannot apply to one thing even by different considerations; (2) a single thing cannot have two genera in one station; (3) essence and essential attributes cannot differ by considerations. The author responds on behalf of al-Sayyid that he meant accidental categories, not real. He also notes that kalam nafsi (inner speech) is from the category of quality, not action or passion, which avoids the problem. The passage thus defends the Hanafi position that ijab and wujub are one in essence while distinct in consideration.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ijab): The act of making an obligation; also the quality of being obligatory. In this passage, it is considered as falling under the category of action (fi‘l).
وجوب (wujub): The necessity or bindingness of a duty; the passive aspect of obligation. In this passage, it is under the category of passion (infi‘al).
مقولة (maqula): A category of being; a highest genus. In this passage, the ten Aristotelian categories are referred to, distinguishing real categories (maqulat haqiqiya) and accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya).
اعتبار (i'tibar): Consideration or aspect; the mental perspective that allows a single entity to be viewed under different categories without multiplying its essence.
مقولة حقيقية (maqula haqiqiya): A real category; a true genus that constitutes the essence of a thing. In this passage, real categories are mutually exclusive and cannot co-apply to a single essence.
مقولة اعتبارية (maqula i'tibariya): An accidental category; a mental consideration that does not affect the essence. Multiple accidental categories can apply to a single thing by different considerations.
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): Inner speech; the eternal attribute of speech in God's essence. It is distinct from expressed speech (kalam lafzi). In this passage, it belongs to the category of quality (kayf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the categories (maqulat) relate to the unity of ijab and wujub?
A) Real categories (maqulat haqiqiya) can apply to a single thing by different considerations, allowing ijab and wujub to be one essence.
B) Accidental categories (maqulat i'tibariya) can apply to a single thing by different considerations, allowing ijab and wujub to be one essence.
C) Both real and accidental categories prevent a single thing from being under two categories, so ijab and wujub must be separate.
D) The categories are irrelevant because ijab and wujub are purely linguistic terms.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends the unity of ijab and wujub by distinguishing real from accidental categories, allowing a single essence to be considered under different categories without contradiction.
